To try to describe the rage of that old man would profit\r\nnothing, the attempt would fall so far short of the purpose. However\r\nwhen he had worn himself out and got quiet again, he looked the matter\r\nover and decided that the twins had some moral rights, although they\r\nhad no legal ones; they were of his blood, and it could not be decorous\r\nto treat them as common clay. So he laid them with their majestic kin\r\nin the Cholmondeley church, with imposing state and ceremony, and added\r\nthe supreme touch by officiating as chief mourner himself. But he drew\r\nthe line at hatchments.\r\n\r\nOur friends in Washington watched the weary days go by, while they\r\nwaited for Pete and covered his name with reproaches because of his\r\ncalamitous procrastinations. Meantime, Sally Sellers, who was as\r\npractical and democratic as the Lady Gwendolen Sellers was romantic and\r\naristocratic, was leading a life of intense interest and activity and\r\ngetting the most she could out of her double personality. All day long\r\nin the privacy of her work-room, Sally Sellers earned bread for the\r\nSellers family; and all the evening Lady Gwendolen Sellers supported\r\nthe Rossmore dignity. All day she was American, practically, and proud\r\nof the work of her head and hands and its commercial result; all the\r\nevening she took holiday and dwelt in a rich shadow-land peopled with\r\ntitled and coroneted fictions. By day, to her, the place was a plain,\r\nunaffected, ramshackle old trap\xe2\x80\x94 just that, and nothing more; by night\r\nit was Rossmore Towers. At college she had learned a trade without\r\nknowing it. The girls had found out that she was the designer of her\r\nown gowns. She had no idle moments after that, and wanted none; for the\r\nexercise of an extraordinary gift is the supremest pleasure in life,\r\nand it was manifest that Sally Sellers possessed a gift of that sort in\r\nthe matter of costume-designing. Within three days after reaching home\r\nshe had hunted up some work; before Pete was yet due in Washington, and\r\nbefore the twins were fairly asleep in English soil, she was already\r\nnearly swamped with work, and the sacrificing of the family chromos for\r\ndebt had got an effective check.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99s a brick,\xe2\x80\x9d said Rossmore to the Major; \xe2\x80\x9cjust her father all over:\r\nprompt to labor with head or hands, and not ashamed of it; capable,\r\nalways capable, let the enterprise be what it may; successful by\r\nnature\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t know what defeat is; thus, intensely and practically\r\nAmerican by inhaled nationalism, and at the same time intensely and\r\naristocratically European by inherited nobility of blood. Just me,\r\nexactly: Mulberry Sellers in matter of finance and invention; after\r\noffice hours, what do you find? The same clothes, yes, but what\xe2\x80\x99s in\r\nthem? Rossmore of the peerage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two friends had haunted the general post-office daily. At last they\r\nhad their reward. Toward evening on the 20th of May, they got a letter\r\nfor XYZ. It bore the Washington postmark; the note itself was not\r\ndated. It said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsh barrel back of lamp post Black horse Alley. If you are playing\r\nsquare go and set on it to-morrow morning 21st 10.22 not sooner not\r\nlater wait till I come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThe friends cogitated over the note profoundly. Presently the earl\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you reckon he\xe2\x80\x99s afraid we are a sheriff with a requisition?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, m\xe2\x80\x99lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause that\xe2\x80\x99s no place for a seance. Nothing friendly, nothing\r\nsociable about it. And at the same time, a body that wanted to know who\r\nwas roosting on that ash-barrel without exposing himself by going near\r\nit, or seeming to be interested in it, could just stand on the street\r\ncorner and take a glance down the alley and satisfy himself, don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nsee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, his idea is plain, now. He seems to be a man that can\xe2\x80\x99t _be_\r\ncandid and straightforward. He acts as if he thought we\xe2\x80\x94shucks, I wish\r\nhe had come out like a man and told us what hotel he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99ve struck it! you\xe2\x80\x99ve struck it sure, Washington; he has told\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHas he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he has; but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to. That alley is a lonesome little\r\npocket that runs along one side of the New Gadsby. That\xe2\x80\x99s his hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes\xe2\x80\x99 you think that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I just know it. He\xe2\x80\x99s got a room that\xe2\x80\x99s just across from that lamp\r\npost. He\xe2\x80\x99s going to sit there perfectly comfortable behind his shutters\r\nat 10.22 to-morrow, and when he sees us sitting on the ash-barrel,\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll say to himself, \xe2\x80\x98I saw _one_ of those fellows on the train\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nthen he\xe2\x80\x99ll pack his satchel in half a minute and ship for the ends of\r\nthe earth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHawkins turned sick with disappointment:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear, it\xe2\x80\x99s all up, Colonel\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s exactly what he\xe2\x80\x99ll do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed he won\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t he? Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause _you_ won\xe2\x80\x99t be holding the ash barrel down, it\xe2\x80\x99ll be me.\r\nYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be coming in with an officer and a requisition in plain\r\nclothes\xe2\x80\x94the officer, I mean\xe2\x80\x94the minute you see him arrive and open up a\r\ntalk with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what a head you have got, Colonel Sellers! I never should have\r\nthought of that in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither would any earl of Rossmore, betwixt William\xe2\x80\x99s contribution and\r\nMulberry\xe2\x80\x94_as_ earl; but it\xe2\x80\x99s office hours, now, you see, and the earl\r\nin me sleeps. Come\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you his very room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey reached the neighborhood of the New Gadsby about nine in the\r\nevening, and passed down the alley to the lamp post.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere you are,\xe2\x80\x9d said the colonel, triumphantly, with a wave of his\r\nhand which took in the whole side of the hotel. \xe2\x80\x9cThere it is\xe2\x80\x94what did I\r\ntell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, but\xe2\x80\x94why, Colonel, it\xe2\x80\x99s six stories high. I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite make out\r\nwhich window you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll the windows, all of them. Let him have his choice\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m indifferent,\r\nnow that I have located him. You go and stand on the corner and wait;\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll prospect the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe earl drifted here and there through the swarming lobby, and finally\r\ntook a waiting position in the neighborhood of the elevator. During an\r\nhour crowds went up and crowds came down; and all complete as to limbs;\r\nbut at last the watcher got a glimpse of a figure that was\r\nsatisfactory\xe2\x80\x94got a glimpse of the back of it, though he had missed his\r\nchance at the face through waning alertness. The glimpse revealed a\r\ncowboy hat, and below it a plaided sack of rather loud pattern, and an\r\nempty sleeve pinned up to the shoulder. Then the elevator snatched the\r\nvision aloft and the watcher fled away in joyful excitement, and\r\nrejoined the fellow-conspirator.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got him, Major\xe2\x80\x94got him sure! I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen him\xe2\x80\x94seen him good; and I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t care where or when that man approaches me backwards, I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nrecognize him every time. We\xe2\x80\x99re all right. Now for the requisition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey got it, after the delays usual in such cases. By half past eleven\r\nthey were at home and happy, and went to bed full of dreams of the\r\nmorrow\xe2\x80\x99s great promise.\r\n\r\nAmong the elevator load which had the suspect for fellow-passenger was\r\na young kinsman of Mulberry Sellers, but Mulberry was not aware of it\r\nand didn\xe2\x80\x99t see him. It was Viscount Berkeley.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII.\r\n\r\n\r\nArrived in his room Lord Berkeley made preparations for that first and\r\nlast and all-the-time duty of the visiting Englishman\xe2\x80\x94the jotting down\r\nin his diary of his \xe2\x80\x9cimpressions\xe2\x80\x9d to date. His preparations consisted\r\nin ransacking his \xe2\x80\x9cbox\xe2\x80\x9d for a pen. There was a plenty of steel pens on\r\nhis table with the ink bottle, but he was English. The English people\r\nmanufacture steel pens for nineteen-twentieths of the globe, but they\r\nnever use any themselves. They use exclusively the pre-historic quill.\r\nMy lord not only found a quill pen, but the best one he had seen in\r\nseveral years\xe2\x80\x94and after writing diligently for some time, closed with\r\nthe following entry:\r\n\r\np071.jpg (18K)\r\n\r\nBUT IN ONE THING I HAVE MADE AN IMMENSE MISTAKE, I OUGHT TO HAVE\r\nSHUCKED MY TITLE AND CHANGED MY NAME BEFORE I STARTED.\r\n\r\nHe sat admiring that pen a while, and then went on:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll attempts to mingle with the common people and become permanently\r\none of them are going to fail, unless I can get rid of it, disappear\r\nfrom it, and re-appear with the solid protection of a new name. I am\r\nastonished and pained to see how eager the most of these Americans are\r\nto get acquainted with a lord, and how diligent they are in pushing\r\nattentions upon him. They lack English servility, it is true\xe2\x80\x94but they\r\ncould acquire it, with practice. My quality travels ahead of me in the\r\nmost mysterious way. I write my family name without additions, on the\r\nregister of this hotel, and imagine that I am going to pass for an\r\nobscure and unknown wanderer, but the clerk promptly calls out, \xe2\x80\x98Front!\r\nshow his lordship to four-eighty-two!\xe2\x80\x99 and before I can get to the lift\r\nthere is a reporter trying to interview me as they call it. This sort\r\nof thing shall cease at once. I will hunt up the American Claimant the\r\nfirst thing in the morning, accomplish my mission, then change my\r\nlodging and vanish from scrutiny under a fictitious name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nHe left his diary on the table, where it would be handy in case any new\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cimpressions\xe2\x80\x9d should wake him up in the night, then he went to bed and\r\npresently fell asleep. An hour or two passed, and then he came slowly\r\nto consciousness with a confusion of mysterious and augmenting sounds\r\nhammering at the gates of his brain for admission; the next moment he\r\nwas sharply awake, and those sounds burst with the rush and roar and\r\nboom of an undammed freshet into his ears. Banging and slamming of\r\nshutters; smashing of windows and the ringing clash of falling glass;\r\nclatter of flying feet along the halls; shrieks, supplications, dumb\r\nmoanings of despair, within, hoarse shouts of command outside;\r\ncracklings and snappings, and the windy roar of victorious flames!\r\n\r\nBang, bang, bang! on the door, and a cry:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurn out\xe2\x80\x94the house is on fire!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cry passed on, and the banging. Lord Berkeley sprang out of bed and\r\nmoved with all possible speed toward the clothes-press in the darkness\r\nand the gathering smoke, but fell over a chair and lost his bearings.\r\nHe groped desperately about on his hands, and presently struck his head\r\nagainst the table and was deeply grateful, for it gave him his bearings\r\nagain, since it stood close by the door. He seized his most precious\r\npossession; his journaled Impressions of America, and darted from the\r\nroom.\r\n\r\n\r\np074.jpg (34K)\r\n\r\nHe ran down the deserted hall toward the red lamp which he knew\r\nindicated the place of a fire-escape. The door of the room beside it\r\nwas open. In the room the gas was burning full head; on a chair was a\r\npile of clothing. He ran to the window, could not get it up, but\r\nsmashed it with a chair, and stepped out on the landing of the\r\nfire-escape; below him was a crowd of men, with a sprinkling of women\r\nand youth, massed in a ruddy light. Must he go down in his spectral\r\nnight dress? No\xe2\x80\x94this side of the house was not yet on fire except at\r\nthe further end; he would snatch on those clothes. Which he did. They\r\nfitted well enough, though a trifle loosely, and they were just a shade\r\nloud as to pattern. Also as to hat\xe2\x80\x94which was of a new breed to him,\r\nBuffalo Bill not having been to England yet. One side of the coat went\r\non, but the other side refused; one of its sleeves was turned up and\r\nstitched to the shoulder. He started down without waiting to get it\r\nloose, made the trip successfully, and was promptly hustled outside the\r\nlimit-rope by the police.\r\n\r\nThe cowboy hat and the coat but half on made him too much of a centre\r\nof attraction for comfort, although nothing could be more profoundly\r\nrespectful, not to say deferential, than was the manner of the crowd\r\ntoward him. In his mind he framed a discouraged remark for early entry\r\nin his diary: \xe2\x80\x9cIt is of no use; they know a lord through any disguise,\r\nand show awe of him\xe2\x80\x94even something very like fear, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently one of the gaping and adoring half-circle of boys ventured a\r\ntimid question. My lord answered it. The boys glanced wonderingly at\r\neach other and from somewhere fell the comment:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_English_ cowboy! Well, if that ain\xe2\x80\x99t curious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother mental note to be preserved for the diary: \xe2\x80\x9cCowboy. Now what\r\nmight a cowboy be? Perhaps\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d But the viscount perceived that some more\r\nquestions were about to be asked; so he worked his way out of the\r\ncrowd, released the sleeve, put on the coat and wandered away to seek a\r\nhumble and obscure lodging. He found it and went to bed and was soon\r\nasleep.\r\n\r\nIn the morning, he examined his clothes. They were rather assertive, it\r\nseemed to him, but they were new and clean, at any rate. There was\r\nconsiderable property in the pockets. Item, five one-hundred dollar\r\nbills. Item, near fifty dollars in small bills and silver. Plug of\r\ntobacco. Hymn-book, which refuses to open; found to contain whiskey.\r\nMemorandum book bearing no name. Scattering entries in it, recording in\r\na sprawling, ignorant hand, appointments, bets, horse-trades, and so\r\non, with people of strange, hyphenated name\xe2\x80\x94Six-Fingered Jake,\r\nYoung-Man-afraid-of-his-Shadow, and the like. No letters, no documents.\r\n\r\nThe young man muses\xe2\x80\x94maps out his course. His letter of credit is\r\nburned; he will borrow the small bills and the silver in these pockets,\r\napply part of it to advertising for the owner, and use the rest for\r\nsustenance while he seeks work. He sends out for the morning paper,\r\nnext, and proceeds to read about the fire. The biggest line in the\r\ndisplay-head announces his own death! The body of the account furnishes\r\nall the particulars; and tells how, with the inherited heroism of his\r\ncaste, he went on saving women and children until escape for himself\r\nwas impossible; then with the eyes of weeping multitudes upon him, he\r\nstood with folded arms and sternly awaited the approach of the\r\ndevouring fiend; \xe2\x80\x9cand so standing, amid a tossing sea of flame and\r\non-rushing billows of smoke, the noble young heir of the great house of\r\nRossmore was caught up in a whirlwind of fiery glory, and disappeared\r\nforever from the vision of men.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe thing was so fine and generous and knightly that it brought the\r\nmoisture to his eyes. Presently he said to himself: \xe2\x80\x9cWhat to do is as\r\nplain as day, now. My Lord Berkeley is dead\xe2\x80\x94let him stay so. Died\r\ncreditably, too; that will make the calamity the easier for my father.\r\nAnd I don\xe2\x80\x99t have to report to the American Claimant, now. Yes, nothing\r\ncould be better than the way matters have turned out. I have only to\r\nfurnish myself with a new name, and take my new start in life totally\r\nuntrammeled. Now I breathe my first breath of real freedom; and how\r\nfresh and breezy and inspiring it is! At last I am a man! a man on\r\nequal terms with my neighbor; and by my manhood, and by it alone, I\r\nshall rise and be seen of the world, or I shall sink from sight and\r\ndeserve it. This _is_ the gladdest day, and the proudest, that ever\r\npoured it\xe2\x80\x99s sun upon my head!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod bless my soul, Hawkins!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe morning paper dropped from the Colonel\xe2\x80\x99s nerveless-grasp.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s gone!\xe2\x80\x94the bright, the young, the gifted, the noblest of his\r\nillustrious race\xe2\x80\x94gone! gone up in flames and unimaginable glory!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy precious, precious young kinsman\xe2\x80\x94Kirkcudbright Llanover\r\nMarjoribanks Sellers Viscount Berkeley, son and heir of usurping\r\nRossmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s true\xe2\x80\x94too true.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight here in Washington; where he arrived from England last night,\r\nthe papers say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHotel burned down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat hotel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe New Gadsby!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my goodness! And have we lost both of them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoth _who?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne-Arm Pete.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, great guns, I forgot all about him. Oh, I hope not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHope! Well, I should say! Oh, we _can\xe2\x80\x99t_ spare _him!_ We can better\r\nafford to lose a million viscounts than our only support and stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey searched the paper diligently, and were appalled to find that a\r\none-armed man had been seen flying along one of the halls of the hotel\r\nin his underclothing and apparently out of his head with fright, and as\r\nhe would listen to no one and persisted in making for a stairway which\r\nwould carry him to certain death, his case was given over as a hopeless\r\none.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor fellow,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Hawkins; \xe2\x80\x9cand he had friends so near. I wish we\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t come away from there\xe2\x80\x94maybe we could have saved him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe earl looked up and said calmly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis being dead doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter. He was uncertain before. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got him\r\nsure, this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot him? How?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will materialize him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRossmore, don\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t trifle with me. Do you mean that? Can you do\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can do it, just as sure as you are sitting there. And I will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me your hand, and let me have the comfort of shaking it. I was\r\nperishing, and you have put new life into me. Get at it, oh, get at it\r\nright away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will take a little time, Hawkins, but there\xe2\x80\x99s no hurry, none in the\r\nworld\xe2\x80\x94in the circumstances. And of course certain duties have devolved\r\nupon me now, which necessarily claim my first attention. This poor\r\nyoung nobleman\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, yes, I am sorry for my heartlessness, and you smitten with this\r\nnew family affliction. Of course you must materialize him first\xe2\x80\x94I quite\r\nunderstand that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94well, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t meaning just that, but,\xe2\x80\x94why, what am I thinking of!\r\nOf course I must materialize him. Oh, Hawkins, selfishness is the\r\nbottom trait in human nature; I was only thinking that now, with the\r\nusurper\xe2\x80\x99s heir out of the way. But you\xe2\x80\x99ll forgive that momentary\r\nweakness, and forget it. Don\xe2\x80\x99t ever remember it against me that\r\nMulberry Sellers was once mean enough to think the thought that I was\r\nthinking. I\xe2\x80\x99ll materialise him\xe2\x80\x94I will, on my honor\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99d do it were\r\nhe a thousand heirs jammed into one and stretching in a solid rank from\r\nhere to the stolen estates of Rossmore, and barring the road forever to\r\nthe rightful earl!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere spoke the real Sellers\xe2\x80\x94the other had a false ring, old friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHawkins, my boy, it just occurs to me\xe2\x80\x94a thing I keep forgetting to\r\nmention\xe2\x80\x94a matter that we\xe2\x80\x99ve got to be mighty careful about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must keep absolutely still about these materializations. Mind, not\r\na hint of them must escape\xe2\x80\x94not a hint. To say nothing of how my wife\r\nand daughter\xe2\x80\x94high-strung, sensitive organizations\xe2\x80\x94might feel about\r\nthem, the negroes wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay on the place a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s true, they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. It\xe2\x80\x99s well you spoke, for I\xe2\x80\x99m not naturally\r\ndiscreet with my tongue when I\xe2\x80\x99m not warned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSellers reached out and touched a bell-button in the wall; set his eye\r\nupon the rear door and waited; touched it again and waited; and just as\r\nHawkins was remarking admiringly that the Colonel was the most\r\nprogressive and most alert man he had ever seen, in the matter of\r\nimpressing into his service every modern convenience the moment it was\r\ninvented, and always keeping breast to breast with the drum major in\r\nthe great work of material civilization, he forsook the button (which\r\nhadn\xe2\x80\x99t any wire attached to it,) rang a vast dinner bell which stood on\r\nthe table, and remarked that he had tried that new-fangled dry battery,\r\nnow, to his entire satisfaction, and had got enough of it; and added:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing would do Graham Bell but I must try it; said the mere _fact_\r\nof my trying it would secure public confidence, and get it a chance to\r\nshow what it could do. I _told_ him that in theory a dry battery was\r\njust a curled darling and no mistake, but when it come to _practice_,\r\nsho!\xe2\x80\x94and here\xe2\x80\x99s the result. Was I right? What should _you_ say,\r\nWashington Hawkins? You\xe2\x80\x99ve seen me try that button twice. Was I\r\nright?\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the idea. Did I know what I was talking about, or didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nI?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you know how I feel about you, Colonel Sellers, and always have\r\nfelt. It seems to me that you always know everything about\r\n_every_thing. If that man had known you as I know you he would have\r\ntaken your judgment at the start, and dropped his dry battery where it\r\nwas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ring, Marse Sellers?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Marse Sellers didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDen it was you, Marse Washington. I\xe2\x80\x99s heah, suh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Marse Washington, either.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDe good lan\xe2\x80\x99! who did ring her, den?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord Rossmore rang it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old negro flung up his hands and exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBlame my skin if I hain\xe2\x80\x99t gone en forgit dat name agin! Come heah,\r\nJinny\xe2\x80\x94run heah, honey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJinny arrived.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou take dish-yer order de lord gwine to give you I\xe2\x80\x99s gwine down\r\nsuller and study dat name tell I git it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take de order! Who\xe2\x80\x99s yo\xe2\x80\x99 nigger las\xe2\x80\x99 year? De bell rung for _you_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDat don\xe2\x80\x99t make no diffunce. When a bell ring for anybody, en old\r\nmarster tell me to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClear out, and settle it in the kitchen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe noise of the quarreling presently sank to a murmur in the distance,\r\nand the earl added: \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a trouble with old house servants that were\r\nyour slaves once and have been your personal friends always.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and members of the family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMembers of the family is just what they become\xe2\x80\x94_the_ members of the\r\nfamily, in fact. And sometimes master and mistress of the household.\r\nThese two are mighty good and loving and faithful and honest, but hang\r\nit, they do just about as they please, they chip into a conversation\r\nwhenever they want to, and the plain fact is, they ought to be killed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a random remark, but it gave him an idea\xe2\x80\x94however, nothing could\r\nhappen without that result.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I wanted, Hawkins, was to send for the family and break the news\r\nto them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, never mind bothering with the servants, then. I will go and bring\r\nthem down.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhile he was gone, the earl worked his idea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen I\xe2\x80\x99ve got the materializing down to a\r\ncertainty, I will get Hawkins to kill them, and after that they will be\r\nunder better control. Without doubt a materialized negro could easily\r\nbe hypnotized into a state resembling silence. And this could be made\r\npermanent\xe2\x80\x94yes, and also modifiable, at will\xe2\x80\x94sometimes _very_ silent,\r\nsometimes turn on more talk, more action, more emotion, according to\r\nwhat you want. It\xe2\x80\x99s a prime good idea. Make it adjustable\xe2\x80\x94with a screw\r\nor something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two ladies entered, now, with Hawkins, and the two negroes\r\nfollowed, uninvited, and fell to brushing and dusting around, for they\r\nperceived that there was matter of interest to the fore, and were\r\nwilling to find out what it was.\r\n\r\nSellers broke the news with stateliness and ceremony, first warning the\r\nladies, with gentle art, that a pang of peculiar sharpness was about to\r\nbe inflicted upon their hearts\xe2\x80\x94hearts still sore from a like hurt,\r\nstill lamenting a like loss\xe2\x80\x94then he took the paper, and with trembling\r\nlips and with tears in his voice he gave them that heroic\r\ndeath-picture.\r\n\r\nThe result was a very genuine outbreak of sorrow and sympathy from all\r\nthe hearers. The elder lady cried, thinking how proud that\r\ngreat-hearted young hero\xe2\x80\x99s mother would be, if she were living, and how\r\nunappeasable her grief; and the two old servants cried with her, and\r\nspoke out their applauses and their pitying lamentations with the\r\neloquent sincerity and simplicity native to their race. Gwendolen was\r\ntouched, and the romantic side of her nature was strongly wrought upon.\r\nShe said that such a nature as that young man\xe2\x80\x99s was rarely and truly\r\nnoble, and nearly perfect; and that with nobility of birth added it was\r\nentirely perfect. For such a man she could endure all things, suffer\r\nall things, even to the sacrificing of her life. She wished she could\r\nhave seen him; the slightest, the most momentary contact with such a\r\nspirit would have ennobled her whole character and made ignoble\r\nthoughts and ignoble acts thereafter impossible to her forever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave they found the body, Rossmore?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is, they\xe2\x80\x99ve found several. It must be one of them, but none\r\nof them are recognizable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going down there and identify one of them and send it home to the\r\nstricken father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut papa, did you ever see the young man?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Gwendolen-why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow will you identify it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94well, you know it says none of them are recognizable. I\xe2\x80\x99ll send his\r\nfather one of them\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s probably no choice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGwendolen knew it was not worth while to argue the matter further,\r\nsince her father\xe2\x80\x99s mind was made up and there was a chance for him to\r\nappear upon that sad scene down yonder in an authentic and official\r\nway. So she said no more\xe2\x80\x94till he asked for a basket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA basket, papa? What for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might be ashes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe earl and Washington started on the sorrowful errand, talking as\r\nthey walked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd as _usual!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, Colonel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeven of them in that hotel. Actresses. And all burnt out, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny of them burnt _up?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no they escaped; they always do; but there\xe2\x80\x99s never a one of them\r\nthat knows enough to fetch out her jewelry with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s strange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStrange\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s the most unaccountable thing in the world. Experience\r\nteaches them nothing; they can\xe2\x80\x99t seem to learn anything except out of a\r\nbook. In some cases there\xe2\x80\x99s manifestly a fatality about it. For\r\ninstance, take What\xe2\x80\x99s-her-name, that plays those sensational thunder\r\nand lightning parts. She\xe2\x80\x99s got a perfectly immense reputation\xe2\x80\x94draws\r\nlike a dog-fight\xe2\x80\x94and it all came from getting burnt out in hotels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, how could that give her a reputation as an actress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94it only made her name familiar. People want to see her play\r\nbecause her name is familiar, but they don\xe2\x80\x99t know what made it\r\nfamiliar, because they don\xe2\x80\x99t remember. First, she was at the bottom of\r\nthe ladder, and absolutely obscure\xe2\x80\x94wages thirteen dollars a week and\r\nfind her own pads.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPads?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94things to fat up her spindles with so as to be plump and\r\nattractive. Well, she got burnt out in a hotel and lost $30,000 worth\r\nof diamonds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe? Where\xe2\x80\x99d she get them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness knows\xe2\x80\x94given to her, no doubt, by spoony young flats and sappy\r\nold bald-heads in the front row. All the papers were full of it. She\r\nstruck for higher pay and got it. Well, she got burnt out again and\r\nlost all her diamonds, and it gave her such a lift that she went\r\nstarring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, if hotel fires are all she\xe2\x80\x99s got to depend on to keep up her\r\nname, it\xe2\x80\x99s a pretty precarious kind of a reputation I should think.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot with her. No, anything but that. Because she\xe2\x80\x99s so lucky; born\r\nlucky, I reckon. Every time there\xe2\x80\x99s a hotel fire she\xe2\x80\x99s in it. She\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nalways there\xe2\x80\x94and if she can\xe2\x80\x99t be there herself, her diamonds are. Now\r\nyou can\xe2\x80\x99t make anything out of that but just sheer luck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never heard of such a thing. She must have lost quarts of diamonds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuarts, she\xe2\x80\x99s lost bushels of them. It\xe2\x80\x99s got so that the hotels are\r\nsuperstitious about her. She\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeen waning a little lately, but this fire will set her up. She lost\r\n$60,000 worth last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think she\xe2\x80\x99s a fool. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is too true, Hawkins\xe2\x80\x94recognition is impossible, not one of the five\r\ncould be identified by its nearest friend. You make the selection, I\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t bear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich one had I better\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, take any of them. Pick out the best one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, the officers assured the earl\xe2\x80\x94for they knew him, everybody in\r\nWashington knew him\xe2\x80\x94that the position in which these bodies were found\r\nmade it impossible that any one of them could be that of his noble\r\nyoung kinsman. They pointed out the spot where, if the newspaper\r\naccount was correct, he must have sunk down to destruction; and at a\r\nwide distance from this spot they showed him where the young man must\r\nhave gone down in case he was suffocated in his room; and they showed\r\nstill a third place, quite remote, where he might possibly have found\r\nhis death if perchance he tried to escape by the side exit toward the\r\nrear. The old Colonel brushed away a tear and said to Hawkins:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs it turns out there was something prophetic in my fears. Yes, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nmatter of ashes. Will you kindly step to a grocery and fetch a couple\r\nmore baskets?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nReverently they got a basket of ashes from each of those now hallowed\r\nspots, and carried them home to consult as to the best manner of\r\nforwarding them to England, and also to give them an opportunity to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9clie in state,\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94a mark of respect which the colonel deemed obligatory,\r\nconsidering the high rank of the deceased.\r\n\r\nThey set the baskets on the table in what was formerly the library,\r\ndrawing-room and workshop\xe2\x80\x94now the Hall of Audience\xe2\x80\x94and went up stairs\r\nto the lumber room to see if they could find a British flag to use as a\r\npart of the outfit proper to the lying in state. A moment later, Lady\r\nRossmore came in from the street and caught sight of the baskets just\r\nas old Jinny crossed her field of vision. She quite lost her patience\r\nand said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what will you do next? What in the world possessed you to\r\nclutter up the parlor table with these baskets of ashes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAshes?\xe2\x80\x9d And she came to look. She put up her hands in pathetic\r\nastonishment. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I _never_ see de like!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t you do it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho, me? Clah to goodness it\xe2\x80\x99s de fust time I\xe2\x80\x99ve sot eyes on \xe2\x80\x99em, Miss\r\nPolly. Dat\xe2\x80\x99s Dan\xe2\x80\x99l. Dat ole moke is losin\xe2\x80\x99 his mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np088.jpg (16K)\r\n\r\nBut it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t Dan\xe2\x80\x99l, for he was called, and denied it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDey ain\xe2\x80\x99t no way to \xe2\x80\x99splain dat. Wen hit\xe2\x80\x99s one er dese-yer common\r\n\xe2\x80\x99currences, a body kin reckon maybe de cat\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d and a shudder shook Lady Rossmore to her foundations. \xe2\x80\x9cI see it\r\nall. Keep away from them\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re _his_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_His_, m\xe2\x80\x99 lady?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94your young Marse Sellers from England that\xe2\x80\x99s burnt up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was alone with the ashes\xe2\x80\x94alone before she could take half a breath.\r\nThen she went after Mulberry Sellers, purposing to make short work of\r\nhis program, whatever it might be; \xe2\x80\x9cfor,\xe2\x80\x9d said she, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen his\r\nsentimentals are up, he\xe2\x80\x99s a numskull, and there\xe2\x80\x99s no knowing what\r\nextravagance he\xe2\x80\x99ll contrive, if you let him alone.\xe2\x80\x9d She found him. He\r\nhad found the flag and was bringing it. When she heard that his idea\r\nwas to have the remains \xe2\x80\x9clie in state, and invite the government and\r\nthe public,\xe2\x80\x9d she broke it up. She said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour intentions are all right\xe2\x80\x94they always are\xe2\x80\x94you want to do honour to\r\nthe remains, and surely nobody can find any fault with that, for he was\r\nyour kin; but you are going the wrong way about it, and you will see it\r\nyourself if you stop and think. You can\xe2\x80\x99t file around a basket of ashes\r\ntrying to look sorry for it and make a sight that is really solemn,\r\nbecause the solemner it is, the more it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94anybody can see that. It\r\nwould be so with one basket; it would be three times so with three.\r\nWell, it stands to reason that if it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be solemn with one\r\nmourner, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be with a procession\xe2\x80\x94and there would be five\r\nthousand people here. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know but it would be pretty near\r\nridiculous; I think it would. No, Mulberry, they can\xe2\x80\x99t lie in state\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nwould be a mistake. Give that up and think of something else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he gave it up; and not reluctantly, when he had thought it over and\r\nrealized how right her instinct was. He concluded to merely sit up with\r\nthe remains just himself and Hawkins. Even this seemed a doubtful\r\nattention, to his wife, but she offered no objection, for it was plain\r\nthat he had a quite honest and simple-hearted desire to do the friendly\r\nand honourable thing by these forlorn poor relics which could command\r\nno hospitality in this far off land of strangers but his. He draped the\r\nflag about the baskets, put some crape on the door-knob, and said with\r\nsatisfaction:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94he is as comfortable, now, as we can make him in the\r\ncircumstances. Except\xe2\x80\x94yes, we must strain a point there\xe2\x80\x94one must do as\r\none would wish to be done by\xe2\x80\x94he must have it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave what, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHatchment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wife felt that the house-front was standing about all it could well\r\nstand, in that way; the prospect of another stunning decoration of that\r\nnature distressed her, and she wished the thing had not occurred to\r\nhim. She said, hesitatingly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought such an honour as that wasn\xe2\x80\x99t allowed to any but very\r\n_very_ near relations, who\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight, you are quite right, my lady, perfectly right; but there aren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nany nearer relatives than relatives by usurpation. We cannot avoid it;\r\nwe are slaves of aristocratic custom and must submit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hatchments were unnecessarily generous, each being as large as a\r\nblanket, and they were unnecessarily volcanic, too, as to variety and\r\nviolence of color, but they pleased the earl\xe2\x80\x99s barbaric eye, and they\r\nsatisfied his taste for symmetry and completeness, too, for they left\r\nno waste room to speak of on the house-front.\r\n\r\nLady Rossmore and her daughter assisted at the sitting-up till near\r\nmidnight, and helped the gentlemen to consider what ought to be done\r\nnext with the remains. Rossmore thought they ought to be sent home with\r\na committee and resolutions,\xe2\x80\x94at once. But the wife was doubtful. She\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould you send all of the baskets?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll at once?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo his father? Oh, no\xe2\x80\x94by no means. Think of the shock. No\xe2\x80\x94one at a\r\ntime; break it to him by degrees.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould _that_ have that effect, father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my daughter. Remember, you are young and elastic, but he is old.\r\nTo send him the whole at once might well be more than he could bear.\r\nBut mitigated\xe2\x80\x94one basket at a time, with restful intervals between, he\r\nwould be used to it by the time he got all of him. And sending him in\r\nthree ships is safer anyway. On account of wrecks and storms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like the idea, father. If I were his father it would be\r\ndreadful to have him coming in that\xe2\x80\x94in that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the installment plan,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Hawkins, gravely, and proud of\r\nbeing able to help.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94dreadful to have him coming in that incoherent way. There would be\r\nthe strain of suspense upon me all the time. To have so depressing a\r\nthing as a funeral impending, delayed, waiting, unaccomplished\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, my child,\xe2\x80\x9d said the earl reassuringly, \xe2\x80\x9cthere would be nothing\r\nof that kind; so old a gentleman could not endure a long-drawn suspense\r\nlike that. There will be three funerals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLady Rossmore looked up surprised, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is _that_ going to make it easier for him? It\xe2\x80\x99s a total mistake,\r\nto my mind. He ought to be buried all at once; I\xe2\x80\x99m sure of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think so, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hawkins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd certainly _I_ should,\xe2\x80\x9d said the daughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are all wrong,\xe2\x80\x9d said the earl. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will see it yourselves, if you\r\nthink. Only one of these baskets has got him in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Rossmore, \xe2\x80\x9cthe thing is perfectly\r\nsimple\xe2\x80\x94bury _that_ one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lady Gwendolen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is _not_ simple,\xe2\x80\x9d said the earl, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause we do not know which\r\nbasket he is in. We know he is in _one_ of them, but that is all we\r\n_do_ know. You see now, I reckon, that I was right; it takes three\r\nfunerals, there is no other way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd three graves and three monuments and three inscriptions?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nthe daughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94yes\xe2\x80\x94to do it right. That is what I should do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt could not be done so, father. Each of the inscriptions would give\r\nthe same name and the same facts and say he was under each and all of\r\nthese monuments, and that would not answer at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe earl nestled uncomfortably in his chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat _is_ an objection. That is a serious objection. I\r\nsee no way out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a general silence for a while. Then Hawkins said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me that if we mixed the three ramifications together\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe earl grasped him by the hand and shook it gratefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt solves the whole problem,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cOne ship, one funeral, one\r\ngrave, one monument\xe2\x80\x94it is admirably conceived. It does you honor, Major\r\nHawkins, it has relieved me of a most painful embarrassment and\r\ndistress, and it will save that poor stricken old father much\r\nsuffering. Yes, he shall go over in one basket.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the wife.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow-immediately, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would wait, Mulberry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait? Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ don\xe2\x80\x99t want to break that childless old man\xe2\x80\x99s heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod knows I don\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen wait till he sends for his son\xe2\x80\x99s remains. If you do that, you\r\nwill never have to give him the last and sharpest pain a parent can\r\nknow\xe2\x80\x94I mean, the _certainty_ that his son is dead. For he will never\r\nsend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy won\xe2\x80\x99t he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause to send\xe2\x80\x94and find out the truth\xe2\x80\x94would rob him of the one\r\nprecious thing left him, the _uncertainty_, the dim hope that maybe,\r\nafter all, his boy escaped, and he will see him again some day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy Polly, he\xe2\x80\x99ll know by the _papers_ that he was burnt up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t let himself _believe_ the papers; he\xe2\x80\x99ll argue against\r\nanything and everything that proves his son is dead; and he will keep\r\nthat up and live on it, and on nothing else till he dies. But if the\r\n_remains_ should actually come, and be put before that poor old\r\ndim-hoping soul\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my God, they never shall! Polly, you\xe2\x80\x99ve saved me from a crime, and\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll bless you for it always. _Now_ we know what to do. We\xe2\x80\x99ll place\r\nthem reverently away, and he shall never know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe young Lord Berkeley, with the fresh air of freedom in his nostrils,\r\nwas feeling invincibly strong for his new career; and yet\xe2\x80\x94and yet\xe2\x80\x94if\r\nthe fight should prove a very hard one at first, very discouraging,\r\nvery taxing on untoughened moral sinews, he might in some weak moment\r\nwant to retreat. Not likely, of course, but possibly that might happen.\r\nAnd so on the whole it might be pardonable caution to burn his bridges\r\nbehind him. Oh, without doubt. He must not stop with advertising for\r\nthe owner of that money, but must put it where he could not borrow from\r\nit himself, meantime, under stress of circumstances. So he went down\r\ntown, and put in his advertisement, then went to a bank and handed in\r\n$500 for deposit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe hesitated and colored a little; he had forgotten to make a\r\nselection. He now brought out the first one that suggested itself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoward Tracy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen he was gone the clerks, marveling, said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe cowboy blushed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe first step was accomplished. The money was still under his command\r\nand at his disposal, but the next step would dispose of that\r\ndifficulty. He went to another bank and drew upon the first bank for\r\nthe $500 by check. The money was collected and deposited a second time\r\nto the credit of Howard Tracy. He was asked to leave a few samples of\r\nhis signature, which he did. Then he went away, once more proud and of\r\nperfect courage, saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo help for me now, for henceforth I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t draw that money without\r\nidentification, and that is become legally impossible. No resources to\r\nfall back on. It is work or starve from now to the end. I am ready\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nnot afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he sent this cablegram to his father:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEscaped unhurt from burning hotel. Have taken fictitious name.\r\nGoodbye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nDuring the evening while he was wandering about in one of the outlying\r\ndistricts of the city, he came across a small brick church, with a bill\r\nposted there with these words printed on it: \xe2\x80\x9cMECHANICS\xe2\x80\x99 CLUB DEBATE.\r\nALL INVITED.\xe2\x80\x9d He saw people, apparently mainly of the working class,\r\nentering the place, and he followed and took his seat. It was a humble\r\nlittle church, quite bare as to ornamentation. It had painted pews\r\nwithout cushions, and no pulpit, properly speaking, but it had a\r\nplatform. On the platform sat the chairman, and by his side sat a man\r\nwho held a manuscript in his hand and had the waiting look of one who\r\nis going to perform the principal part. The church was soon filled with\r\na quiet and orderly congregation of decently dressed and modest people.\r\nThis is what the chairman said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe essayist for this evening is an old member of our club whom you\r\nall know, Mr. Parker, assistant editor of the Daily Democrat. The\r\nsubject of his essay is the American Press, and he will use as his text\r\na couple of paragraphs taken from Mr. Matthew Arnold\xe2\x80\x99s new book. He\r\nasks me to read these texts for him. The first is as follows:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Goethe says somewhere that \xe2\x80\x9cthe thrill of awe,\xe2\x80\x9d that is to say,\r\nREVERENCE, is the best thing humanity has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Arnold\xe2\x80\x99s other paragraph is as follows:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I should say that if one were searching for the best means to efface\r\nand kill in a whole nation the discipline of respect, one could not do\r\nbetter than take the American newspapers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np095.jpg (18K)\r\n\r\nMr. Parker rose and bowed, and was received with warm applause. He then\r\nbegan to read in a good round resonant voice, with clear enunciation\r\nand careful attention to his pauses and emphases. His points were\r\nreceived with approval as he went on.\r\n\r\nThe essayist took the position that the most important function of a\r\npublic journal in any country was the propagating of national feeling\r\nand pride in the national name\xe2\x80\x94the keeping the people \xe2\x80\x9cin love with\r\n_their_ country and _its_ institutions, and shielded from the\r\nallurements of alien and inimical systems.\xe2\x80\x9d He sketched the manner in\r\nwhich the reverent Turkish or Russian journalist fulfilled this\r\nfunction\xe2\x80\x94the one assisted by the prevalent \xe2\x80\x9cdiscipline of respect\xe2\x80\x9d for\r\nthe bastinado, the other for Siberia. Continuing, he said:\r\n\r\nThe chief function of an English journal is that of all other journals\r\nthe world over: it must keep the public eye fixed admiringly upon\r\ncertain things, and keep it diligently diverted from certain others.\r\nFor instance, it must keep the public eye fixed admiringly upon the\r\nglories of England, a processional splendor stretching its receding\r\nline down the hazy vistas of time, with the mellowed lights of a\r\nthousand years glinting from its banners; and it must keep it\r\ndiligently diverted from the fact that all these glories were for the\r\nenrichment and aggrandizement of the petted and privileged few, at cost\r\nof the blood and sweat and poverty of the unconsidered masses who\r\nachieved them but might not enter in and partake of them. It must keep\r\nthe public eye fixed in loving and awful reverence upon the throne as a\r\nsacred thing, and diligently divert it from the fact that no throne was\r\never set up by the unhampered vote of a majority of any nation; and\r\nthat hence no throne exists that has a right to exist, and no symbol of\r\nit, flying from any flagstaff, is righteously entitled to wear any\r\ndevice but the skull and crossbones of that kindred industry which\r\ndiffers from royalty only business-wise\xe2\x80\x94merely as retail differs from\r\nwholesale. It must keep the citizen\xe2\x80\x99s eye fixed in reverent docility\r\nupon that curious invention of machine politics, an Established Church,\r\nand upon that bald contradiction of common justice, a hereditary\r\nnobility; and diligently divert it from the fact that the one damns him\r\nif he doesn\xe2\x80\x99t wear its collar, and robs him under the gentle name of\r\ntaxation whether he wears it or not, and the other gets all the honors\r\nwhile he does all the work.\r\n\r\nThe essayist thought that Mr. Arnold, with his trained eye and\r\nintelligent observation, ought to have perceived that the very quality\r\nwhich he so regretfully missed from our press\xe2\x80\x94respectfulness, reverence\r\n\xe2\x80\x94was exactly the thing which would make our press useless to us if it\r\nhad it\xe2\x80\x94rob it of the very thing which differentiates it from all other\r\njournalism in the world and makes it distinctively and preciously\r\nAmerican, its frank and cheerful irreverence being by all odds the most\r\nvaluable of all its qualities. \xe2\x80\x9cFor its mission\xe2\x80\x94overlooked by Mr.\r\nArnold\xe2\x80\x94is to stand guard over a nation\xe2\x80\x99s liberties, not its humbugs and\r\nshams.\xe2\x80\x9d He thought that if during fifty years the institutions of the\r\nold world could be exposed to the fire of a flouting and scoffing press\r\nlike ours, \xe2\x80\x9cmonarchy and its attendant crimes would disappear from\r\nChristendom.\xe2\x80\x9d Monarchists might doubt this; then \xe2\x80\x9cwhy not persuade the\r\nCzar to give it a trial in Russia?\xe2\x80\x9d Concluding, he said:\r\n\r\nWell, the charge is, that our press has but little of that old world\r\nquality, reverence. Let us be candidly grateful that it is so. With its\r\nlimited reverence it at least reveres the things which this nation\r\nreveres, as a rule, and that is sufficient: what other people revere is\r\nfairly and properly matter of light importance to us. Our press does\r\nnot reverence kings, it does not reverence so called nobilities, it\r\ndoes not reverence established ecclesiastical slaveries, it does not\r\nreverence laws which rob a younger son to fatten an elder one, it does\r\nnot reverence any fraud or sham or infamy, howsoever old or rotten or\r\nholy, which sets one citizen above his neighbor by accident of birth:\r\nit does not reverence any law or custom, howsoever old or decayed or\r\nsacred, which shuts against the best man in the land the best place in\r\nthe land and the divine right to prove property and go up and occupy\r\nit. In the sense of the poet Goethe\xe2\x80\x94that meek idolater of provincial\r\nthree carat royalty and nobility\xe2\x80\x94our press is certainly bankrupt in the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthrill of awe\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94otherwise reverence; reverence for nickel plate and\r\nbrummagem. Let us sincerely hope that this fact will remain a fact\r\nforever: for to my mind a discriminating irreverence is the creator and\r\nprotector of human liberty\xe2\x80\x94even as the other thing is the creator,\r\nnurse, and steadfast protector of all forms of human slavery, bodily\r\nand mental.\r\n\r\nTracy said to himself, almost shouted to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad I came to\r\nthis country. I was right. I was right to seek out a land where such\r\nhealthy principles and theories are in men\xe2\x80\x99s hearts and minds. Think of\r\nthe innumerable slaveries imposed by misplaced reverence! How well he\r\nbrought that out, and how true it is. There\xe2\x80\x99s manifestly prodigious\r\nforce in reverence. If you can get a man to reverence your ideals, he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nyour slave. Oh, yes, in all the ages the peoples of Europe have been\r\ndiligently taught to avoid reasoning about the shams of monarchy and\r\nnobility, been taught to avoid examining them, been taught to reverence\r\nthem; and now, as a natural result, to reverence them is second nature.\r\nIn order to shock them it is sufficient to inject a thought of the\r\nopposite kind into their dull minds. For ages, any expression of\r\nso-called irreverence from their lips has been sin and crime. The sham\r\nand swindle of all this is apparent the moment one reflects that he is\r\nhimself the only legitimately qualified judge of what is entitled to\r\nreverence and what is not. Come, I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t thought of that before, but\r\nit is true, absolutely true. What right has Goethe, what right has\r\nArnold, what right has any dictionary, to define the word Irreverence\r\nfor me? What their ideals are is nothing to me. So long as I reverence\r\nmy own ideals my whole duty is done, and I commit no profanation if I\r\nlaugh at theirs. I may scoff at other people\xe2\x80\x99s ideals as much as I want\r\nto. It is my right and my privilege. No man has any right to deny it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy was expecting to hear the essay debated, but this did not happen.\r\nThe chairman said, by way of explanation:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would say, for the information of the strangers present here, that\r\nin accordance with our custom the subject of this meeting will be\r\ndebated at the next meeting of the club. This is in order to enable our\r\nmembers to prepare what they may wish to say upon the subject with pen\r\nand paper, for we are mainly mechanics and unaccustomed to speaking. We\r\nare obliged to write down what we desire to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMany brief papers were now read, and several offhand speeches made in\r\ndiscussion of the essay read at the last meeting of the club, which had\r\nbeen a laudation, by some visiting professor, of college culture, and\r\nthe grand results flowing from it to the nation. One of the papers was\r\nread by a man approaching middle age, who said he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t had a college\r\neducation, that he had got his education in a printing office, and had\r\ngraduated from there into the patent office, where he had been a clerk\r\nnow for a great many years. Then he continued to this effect:\r\n\r\nThe essayist contrasted the America of to-day with the America of\r\nbygone times, and certainly the result is the exhibition of a mighty\r\nprogress. But I think he a little overrated the college-culture share\r\nin the production of that result. It can no doubt be easily shown that\r\nthe colleges have contributed the intellectual part of this progress,\r\nand that that part is vast; but that the material progress has been\r\nimmeasurably vaster, I think you will concede. Now I have been looking\r\nover a list of inventors\xe2\x80\x94the creators of this amazing material\r\ndevelopment\xe2\x80\x94and I find that they were not college-bred men. Of course\r\nthere are exceptions\xe2\x80\x94like Professor Henry of Princeton, the inventor of\r\nMr. Morse\xe2\x80\x99s system of telegraphy\xe2\x80\x94but these exceptions are few. It is\r\nnot overstatement to say that the imagination-stunning material\r\ndevelopment of this century, the only century worth living in since\r\ntime itself was invented, is the creation of men not college-bred. We\r\nthink we see what these inventors have done: no, we see only the\r\nvisible vast frontage of their work; behind it is their far vaster\r\nwork, and it is invisible to the careless glance. They have\r\nreconstructed this nation\xe2\x80\x94made it over, that is\xe2\x80\x94and metaphorically\r\nspeaking, have multiplied its numbers almost beyond the power of\r\nfigures to express. I will explain what I mean. What constitutes the\r\npopulation of a land? Merely the numberable packages of meat and bones\r\nin it called by courtesy men and women? Shall a million ounces of brass\r\nand a million ounces of gold be held to be of the same value? Take a\r\ntruer standard: the measure of a man\xe2\x80\x99s contributing capacity to his\r\ntime and his people\xe2\x80\x94the work he can do\xe2\x80\x94and then number the population\r\nof this country to-day, as multiplied by what a man can now do, more\r\nthan his grandfather could do. By this standard of measurement, this\r\nnation, two or three generations ago, consisted of mere cripples,\r\nparalytics, dead men, as compared with the men of to-day. In 1840 our\r\npopulation was 17,000,000. By way of rude but striking illustration,\r\nlet us consider, for argument\xe2\x80\x99s sake, that four of these millions\r\nconsisted of aged people, little children, and other incapables, and\r\nthat the remaining 13,000,000 were divided and employed as follows:\r\n\r\n2,000,000 as ginners of cotton.\r\n\r\n6,000,000 (women) as stocking-knitters.\r\n\r\n2,000,000 (women) as thread-spinners.\r\n\r\n500,000 as screw makers.\r\n\r\n400,000 as reapers, binders, etc.\r\n\r\n1,000,000 as corn-shellers.\r\n\r\n40,000 as weavers.\r\n\r\n1,000 as stitchers of shoe soles.\r\n\r\nNow the deductions which I am going to append to these figures may\r\nsound extravagant, but they are not. I take them from Miscellaneous\r\nDocuments No. 50, second session 45th Congress, and they are official\r\nand trustworthy. To-day, the work of those 2,000,000 cotton-ginners is\r\ndone by 2,000 men; that of the 6,000,000 stocking-knitters is done by\r\n3,000 boys; that of the 2,000,000 thread-spinners is done by 1,000\r\ngirls; that of the 500,000 screw makers is done by 500 girls; that of\r\nthe 400,000 reapers, binders, etc., is done by 4,000 boys; that of the\r\n1,000,000 corn-shellers is done by 7,500 men; that of the 40,000\r\nweavers is done by 1,200 men; and that of the 1,000 stitchers of shoe\r\nsoles is done by 6 men. To bunch the figures, 17,900 persons to-day do\r\nthe above-work, whereas fifty years ago it would have taken thirteen\r\nmillions of persons to do it. Now then, how many of that ignorant\r\nrace\xe2\x80\x94our fathers and grandfathers\xe2\x80\x94with their ignorant methods, would it\r\ntake to do our work to-day? It would take forty thousand millions\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nhundred times the swarming population of China\xe2\x80\x94twenty times the present\r\npopulation of the globe. You look around you and you see a nation of\r\nsixty millions\xe2\x80\x94apparently; but secreted in their hands and brains, and\r\ninvisible to your eyes, is the true population of this Republic, and it\r\nnumbers forty billions! It is the stupendous creation of those humble\r\nunlettered, un-college-bred inventors\xe2\x80\x94all honor to their name.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow grand that is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tracy, as he wended homeward. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a\r\ncivilization it is, and what prodigious results these are! and brought\r\nabout almost wholly by common men; not by Oxford-trained aristocrats,\r\nbut men who stand shoulder to shoulder in the humble ranks of life and\r\nearn the bread that they eat. Again, I\xe2\x80\x99m glad I came. I have found a\r\ncountry at last where one may start fair, and breast to breast with his\r\nfellow man, rise by his own efforts, and be something in the world and\r\nbe proud of that something; not be something created by an ancestor\r\nthree hundred years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI.\r\n\r\n\r\nDuring the first few days he kept the fact diligently before his mind\r\nthat he was in a land where there was \xe2\x80\x9cwork and bread for all.\xe2\x80\x9d In\r\nfact, for convenience\xe2\x80\x99 sake he fitted it to a little tune and hummed it\r\nto himself; but as time wore on the fact itself began to take on a\r\ndoubtful look, and next the tune got fatigued and presently ran down\r\nand stopped. His first effort was to get an upper clerkship in one of\r\nthe departments, where his Oxford education could come into play and do\r\nhim service. But he stood no chance whatever. There, competency was no\r\nrecommendation; political backing, without competency, was worth six of\r\nit. He was glaringly English, and that was necessarily against him in\r\nthe political centre of a nation where both parties prayed for the\r\nIrish cause on the house-top and blasphemed it in the cellar. By his\r\ndress he was a cowboy; that won him respect\xe2\x80\x94when his back was not\r\nturned\xe2\x80\x94but it couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get a clerkship for him. But he had said, in a\r\nrash moment, that he would wear those clothes till the owner or the\r\nowner\xe2\x80\x99s friends caught sight of them and asked for that money, and his\r\nconscience would not let him retire from that engagement now.\r\n\r\nAt the end of a week things were beginning to wear rather a startling\r\nlook. He had hunted everywhere for work, descending gradually the scale\r\nof quality, until apparently he had sued for all the various kinds of\r\nwork a man without a special calling might hope to be able to do,\r\nexcept ditching and the other coarse manual sorts\xe2\x80\x94and had got neither\r\nwork nor the promise of it.\r\n\r\nHe was mechanically turning over the leaves of his diary, meanwhile,\r\nand now his eye fell upon the first record made after he was burnt out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI myself did not doubt my stamina before, nobody could doubt it now,\r\nif they could see how I am housed, and realise that I feel absolutely\r\nno disgust with these quarters, but am as serenely content with them as\r\nany dog would be in a similar kennel. Terms, twenty-five dollars a\r\nweek. I said I would start at the bottom. I have kept my word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nA shudder went quaking through him, and he exclaimed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I been thinking of! _This_ the bottom! Mooning along a whole\r\nweek, and these terrific expenses climbing and climbing all the time! I\r\nmust end this folly straightway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe settled up at once and went forth to find less sumptuous lodgings.\r\nHe had to wander far and seek with diligence, but he succeeded. They\r\nmade him pay in advance\xe2\x80\x94four dollars and a half; this secured both bed\r\nand food for a week. The good-natured, hardworked landlady took him up\r\nthree flights of narrow, uncarpeted stairs and delivered him into his\r\nroom. There were two double-bedsteads in it, and one single one. He\r\nwould be allowed to sleep alone in one of the double beds until some\r\nnew boarder should come, but he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be charged extra.\r\n\r\nSo he would presently be required to sleep with some stranger! The\r\nthought of it made him sick. Mrs. Marsh, the landlady, was very\r\nfriendly and hoped he would like her house\xe2\x80\x94they all liked it, she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they\xe2\x80\x99re a very nice set of boys. They carry on a good deal, but\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s their fun. You see, this room opens right into this back one,\r\nand sometimes they\xe2\x80\x99re all in one and sometimes in the other; and hot\r\nnights they all sleep on the roof when it don\xe2\x80\x99t rain. They get out\r\nthere the minute it\xe2\x80\x99s hot enough. The season\xe2\x80\x99s so early that they\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nalready had a night or two up there. If you\xe2\x80\x99d like to go up and pick\r\nout a place, you can. You\xe2\x80\x99ll find chalk in the side of the chimney\r\nwhere there\xe2\x80\x99s a brick wanting. You just take the chalk and\xe2\x80\x94but of\r\ncourse you\xe2\x80\x99ve done it before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I haven\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course you haven\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94what am I thinking of? Plenty of room on\r\nthe Plains without chalking, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be bound. Well, you just chalk out a\r\nplace the size of a blanket anywhere on the tin that ain\xe2\x80\x99t already\r\nmarked off, you know, and that\xe2\x80\x99s your property. You and your bed-mate\r\ntake turnabout carrying up the blanket and pillows and fetching them\r\ndown again; or one carries them up and the other fetches them down, you\r\nfix it the way you like, you know. You\xe2\x80\x99ll like the boys, they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\neverlasting sociable\xe2\x80\x94except the printer. He\xe2\x80\x99s the one that sleeps in\r\nthat single bed\xe2\x80\x94the strangest creature; why, I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you could\r\nget that man to sleep with another man, not if the house was afire.\r\nMind you, I\xe2\x80\x99m not just talking, I know. The boys tried him, to see.\r\nThey took his bed out one night, and so when he got home about three in\r\nthe morning\xe2\x80\x94he was on a morning paper then, but he\xe2\x80\x99s on an evening one\r\nnow\xe2\x80\x94there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any place for him but with the iron-moulder; and if\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ll believe me, he just set up the rest of the night\xe2\x80\x94he did, honest.\r\nThey say he\xe2\x80\x99s cracked, but it ain\xe2\x80\x99t so, he\xe2\x80\x99s English\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re awful\r\nparticular. You won\xe2\x80\x99t mind my saying that. You\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re English?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought so. I could tell it by the way you mispronounce the words\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x99s got a\xe2\x80\x99s in them, you know; such as saying loff when you mean\r\nlaff \xe2\x80\x94but you\xe2\x80\x99ll get over that. He\xe2\x80\x99s a right down good fellow, and a\r\nlittle sociable with the photographer\xe2\x80\x99s boy and the caulker and the\r\nblacksmith that work in the navy yard, but not so much with the others.\r\nThe fact is, though it\xe2\x80\x99s private, and the others don\xe2\x80\x99t know it, he\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nkind of an aristocrat, his father being a doctor, and _you_ know what\r\nstyle _that_ is\xe2\x80\x94in England, I mean, because in this country a doctor\r\nain\xe2\x80\x99t so _very_ much, even if he\xe2\x80\x99s _that_. But over there of course\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s different. So this chap had a falling out with his father, and was\r\npretty high strung, and just cut for this country, and the first he\r\nknew he had to get to work or starve. Well, he\xe2\x80\x99d been to college, you\r\nsee, and so he judged _he_ was all right\xe2\x80\x94did you say anything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I only sighed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd there\xe2\x80\x99s where he was mistaken. Why, he mighty near starved. And I\r\nreckon he would have starved sure enough, if some jour\xe2\x80\x99 printer or\r\nother hadn\xe2\x80\x99t took pity on him and got him a place as apprentice. So he\r\nlearnt the trade, and then he was all right\xe2\x80\x94but it was a close call.\r\nOnce he thought he had _got_ to haul in his pride and holler for his\r\nfather and\xe2\x80\x94why, you\xe2\x80\x99re sighing again. Is anything the matter with\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x94does my clatter\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _dear_\xe2\x80\x94no. Pray go on\xe2\x80\x94I like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you see, he\xe2\x80\x99s been over here ten years; he\xe2\x80\x99s twenty-eight, now,\r\nand he ain\xe2\x80\x99t pretty well satisfied in his mind, because he can\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nreconciled to being a mechanic and associating with mechanics, he\r\nbeing, as he says to me, a _gentleman_, which is a pretty plain\r\nletting-on that the boys ain\xe2\x80\x99t, but of course I know enough not to let\r\n_that_ cat out of the bag.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy\xe2\x80\x94would there be any harm in it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHarm in it? They\xe2\x80\x99d lick him, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they? Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t _you?_ Of course\r\nyou would. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you ever let a man say you ain\xe2\x80\x99t a gentleman in _this_\r\ncountry. But laws, what am I thinking about? I reckon a body would\r\nthink twice before he said a cowboy wasn\xe2\x80\x99t a gentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA trim, active, slender and very pretty girl of about eighteen walked\r\ninto the room now, in the most satisfied and unembarrassed way. She was\r\ncheaply but smartly and gracefully dressed, and the mother\xe2\x80\x99s quick\r\nglance at the stranger\xe2\x80\x99s face as he rose, was of the kind which\r\ninquires what effect has been produced, and expects to find indications\r\nof surprise and admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is my daughter Hattie\xe2\x80\x94we call her Puss. It\xe2\x80\x99s the new boarder,\r\nPuss.\xe2\x80\x9d This without rising.\r\n\r\nThe young Englishman made the awkward bow common to his nationality and\r\ntime of life in circumstances of delicacy and difficulty, and these\r\nwere of that sort; for, being taken by surprise, his natural, lifelong\r\nself sprang to the front, and that self of course would not know just\r\nhow to act when introduced to a chambermaid, or to the heiress of a\r\nmechanics\xe2\x80\x99 boarding house. His other self\xe2\x80\x94the self which recognized the\r\nequality of all men\xe2\x80\x94would have managed the thing better, if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nbeen caught off guard and robbed of its chance. The young girl paid no\r\nattention to the bow, but put out her hand frankly and gave the\r\nstranger a friendly shake and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np109.jpg (23K)\r\n\r\nThen she marched to the one washstand in the room, tilted her head this\r\nway and that before the wreck of a cheap mirror that hung above it,\r\ndampened her fingers with her tongue, perfected the circle of a little\r\nlock of hair that was pasted against her forehead, then began to busy\r\nherself with the slops.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I must be going\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s getting towards supper time. The young man wondered a little\r\nthat a mother who seemed so honest and respectable should be so\r\nthoughtless, and was reaching for his hat, intending to disembarrass\r\nthe girl of his presence; but she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94nowhere in particular, but as I am only in the way here\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, who said you were in the way? Sit down\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll move you when you are\r\nin the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was making the beds, now. He sat down and watched her deft and\r\ndiligent performance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat gave you that notion? We are away up here in an empty\r\nhouse, and your mother being gone\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl interrupted him with an amused laugh, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody to protect me? It\xe2\x80\x99s the same with Maggie Lee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the boarders; young lady that works in the fact\xe2\x80\x99ry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe works in a factory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Shoe factory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a shoe factory; and you call her a young lady?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, she\xe2\x80\x99s only twenty-two; what should you call her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wasn\xe2\x80\x99t thinking of her age, I was thinking of the title. I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry. I hoped you had only men and women; everybody equal; no\r\ndifferences in rank.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl stopped with a pillow in her teeth and the case spread open\r\nbelow it, contemplating him from under her brows with a slightly\r\npuzzled expression. Where\xe2\x80\x99s any difference in rank?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you call a factory girl a young _lady_, what do you call the\r\nPresident\xe2\x80\x99s wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCall her an _old_ one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you make age the only distinction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere ain\xe2\x80\x99t any other to make as far as I can see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen _all_ women are ladies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly they are. All the respectable ones.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that puts a better face on it. Certainly there is no harm in a\r\ntitle when it is given to everybody. It is only an offense and a wrong\r\nwhen it is restricted to a favored few. But Miss\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHattie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Hattie, be frank; confess that that title _isn\xe2\x80\x99t_ accorded by\r\neverybody to everybody. He had\r\ncome from the front room beyond the hall, where he had left his hat,\r\nand he had a chipped and cracked white wash-bowl in his hand. The girl\r\ncame and took the bowl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll get it for you. You go right ahead and _give_ it to him, Mr.\r\nBarrow. He\xe2\x80\x99s the new boarder\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Tracy\xe2\x80\x94and I\xe2\x80\x99d just got to where it was\r\ngetting too deep for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMuch obliged if you will, Hattie.
