You see where you are coming to, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? _Calling_ yourself a\r\nlady doesn\xe2\x80\x99t elect you; that is what you were going to say; and you saw\r\nthat if you said it you were going to run right up against another\r\ndifference that you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t thought of: to-wit, Whose _right_ is it to\r\ndo the electing? Over there, twenty thousand people in a million elect\r\nthemselves gentlemen and ladies, and the nine hundred and eighty\r\nthousand _accept_ that decree and swallow the affront which it puts\r\nupon them. Why, if they didn\xe2\x80\x99t accept it, it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t _be_ an election,\r\nit would be a dead letter and have no force at all. Over here the\r\ntwenty thousand would-be exclusives come up to the polls and vote\r\nthemselves to be ladies and gentlemen. But the thing doesn\xe2\x80\x99t stop\r\nthere. The nine hundred and eighty thousand come and vote themselves to\r\nbe ladies and gentlemen _too_, and that elects the whole nation. Since\r\nthe whole million vote themselves ladies and gentlemen, there is no\r\nquestion about that election. It _does_ make absolute equality, and\r\nthere is no fiction about it; while over yonder the _inequality_, (by\r\ndecree of the infinitely feeble, and consent of the infinitely strong,)\r\nis also absolute\xe2\x80\x94as real and absolute as our equality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy had shrunk promptly into his English shell when this speech\r\nbegan, notwithstanding he had now been in severe training several weeks\r\nfor contact and intercourse with the common herd on the common herd\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nterms; but he lost no time in pulling himself out again, and so by the\r\ntime the speech was finished his valves were open once more, and he was\r\nforcing himself to accept without resentment the common herd\xe2\x80\x99s frank\r\nfashion of dropping sociably into other people\xe2\x80\x99s conversations\r\nunembarrassed and uninvited. The process was not very difficult this\r\ntime, for the man\xe2\x80\x99s smile and voice and manner were persuasive and\r\nwinning. Tracy would even have liked him on the spot, but for the\r\nfact\xe2\x80\x94fact which he was not really aware of\xe2\x80\x94that the equality of men was\r\nnot yet a reality to him, it was only a theory; the _mind_ perceived,\r\nbut the _man_ failed to feel it. It was Hattie\xe2\x80\x99s ghost over again,\r\nmerely turned around. Theoretically Barrow was his equal, but it was\r\ndistinctly distasteful to see him exhibit it. He presently said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope in all sincerity that what you have said is true, as regards\r\nthe Americans, for doubts have crept into my mind several times. It\r\nseemed that the equality must be ungenuine where the sign-names of\r\ncastes were still in vogue; but those sign-names have certainly lost\r\ntheir offence and are wholly neutralized, nullified and harmless if\r\nthey are the undisputed property of every individual in the nation. I\r\nthink I realize that caste does not exist and cannot exist except by\r\ncommon consent of the masses outside of its limits. I thought caste\r\ncreated itself and perpetuated itself; but it seems quite true that it\r\nonly creates itself, and is perpetuated by the people whom it despises,\r\nand who can dissolve it at any time by assuming its mere sign-names\r\nthemselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s what I think. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t any power on earth that can prevent\r\nEngland\xe2\x80\x99s thirty millions from electing themselves dukes and duchesses\r\nto-morrow and calling themselves so. And within six months all the\r\nformer dukes and duchesses would have retired from the business. I wish\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99d try that. Royalty itself couldn\xe2\x80\x99t survive such a process. A\r\nhandful of frowners against thirty million laughers in a state of\r\nirruption. Why, it\xe2\x80\x99s Herculaneum against Vesuvius; it would take\r\nanother eighteen centuries to find that Herculaneum after the\r\ncataclysm. What\xe2\x80\x99s a Colonel in our South? He\xe2\x80\x99s a nobody; because\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99re all colonels down there. No, Tracy\xe2\x80\x9d (shudder from Tracy)\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cnobody in England would call you a gentleman and you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t call\r\nyourself one; and I tell you it\xe2\x80\x99s a state of things that makes a man\r\nput himself into most unbecoming attitudes sometimes\xe2\x80\x94the broad and\r\ngeneral recognition and acceptance of caste _as_ caste does, I mean.\r\nMakes him do it unconsciously\xe2\x80\x94being bred in him, you see, and never\r\nthought over and reasoned out. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t conceive of the Matterhorn\r\nbeing flattered by the notice of one of your comely little English\r\nhills, could you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, let a man in his right mind try to conceive of Darwin\r\nfeeling flattered by the notice of a princess. It\xe2\x80\x99s so grotesque that\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94well, it paralyzes the imagination. Yet that Memnon _was_ flattered\r\nby the notice of that statuette; he _says_ so\xe2\x80\x94says so himself. The\r\nsystem that can make a god disown his godship and profane it\xe2\x80\x94oh, well,\r\nit\xe2\x80\x99s all wrong, it\xe2\x80\x99s all wrong and ought to be abolished, I should\r\nsay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe mention of Darwin brought on a literary discussion, and this topic\r\nroused such enthusiasm in Barrow that he took off his coat and made\r\nhimself the more free and comfortable for it, and detained him so long\r\nthat he was still at it when the noisy proprietors of the room came\r\nshouting and skylarking in and began to romp, scuffle, wash, and\r\notherwise entertain themselves. He lingered yet a little longer to\r\noffer the hospitalities of his room and his book shelf to Tracy and ask\r\nhim a personal question or two:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your trade?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x94well, they call me a cowboy, but that is a fancy. I\xe2\x80\x99m not that. I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t any trade.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you work at for your living?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, anything\xe2\x80\x94I mean I _would_ work at, anything I could get to do, but\r\nthus far I haven\xe2\x80\x99t been able to find an occupation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMaybe I can help you; I\xe2\x80\x99d like to try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be very glad. I\xe2\x80\x99ve tried, myself, to weariness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of course where a man hasn\xe2\x80\x99t a regular trade he\xe2\x80\x99s pretty bad off\r\nin this world. What you needed, I reckon, was less book learning and\r\nmore bread-and-butter learning. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what your father could\r\nhave been thinking of. You ought to have had a trade, you ought to have\r\nhad a trade, by _all_ means. But never mind about that; we\xe2\x80\x99ll stir up\r\n_something_ to do, I guess. And don\xe2\x80\x99t you get homesick; that\xe2\x80\x99s a bad\r\nbusiness. We\xe2\x80\x99ll talk the thing over and look around a little. You\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\ncome out all right. Wait for me\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll go down to supper with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time Tracy had achieved a very friendly feeling for Barrow and\r\nwould have _called_ him a friend, maybe, if not taken too suddenly on a\r\nstraight-out requirement to realize on his theories. He was glad of his\r\nsociety, anyway, and was feeling lighter hearted than before. Also he\r\nwas pretty curious to know what vocation it might be which had\r\nfurnished Barrow such a large acquaintanceship with books and allowed\r\nhim so much time to read.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII.\r\n\r\n\r\nPresently the supper bell began to ring in the depths of the house, and\r\nthe sound proceeded steadily upward, growing in intensity all the way\r\nup towards the upper floors. The higher it came the more maddening was\r\nthe noise, until at last what it lacked of being absolutely deafening,\r\nwas made up of the sudden crash and clatter of an avalanche of boarders\r\ndown the uncarpeted stairway. The peerage did not go to meals in this\r\nfashion; Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s training had not fitted him to enjoy this hilarious\r\nzoological clamor and enthusiasm. He had to confess that there was\r\nsomething about this extraordinary outpouring of animal spirits which\r\nhe would have to get inured to before he could accept it. No doubt in\r\ntime he would prefer it; but he wished the process might be modified\r\nand made just a little more gradual, and not quite so pronounced and\r\nviolent. Barrow and Tracy followed the avalanche down through an ever\r\nincreasing and ever more and more aggressive stench of bygone cabbage\r\nand kindred smells; smells which are to be found nowhere but in a cheap\r\nprivate boarding house; smells which once encountered can never be\r\nforgotten; smells which encountered generations later are instantly\r\nrecognizable, but never recognizable with pleasure. To Tracy these\r\nodors were suffocating, horrible, almost unendurable; but he held his\r\npeace and said nothing. Arrived in the basement, they entered a large\r\ndining-room where thirty-five or forty people sat at a long table. They\r\ntook their places. The feast had already begun and the conversation was\r\ngoing on in the liveliest way from one end of the table to the other.\r\nThe table cloth was of very coarse material and was liberally spotted\r\nwith coffee stains and grease. The knives and forks were iron, with\r\nbone handles, the spoons appeared to be iron or sheet iron or something\r\nof the sort. The tea and coffee cups were of the commonest and heaviest\r\nand most durable stone ware. All the furniture of the table was of the\r\ncommonest and cheapest sort. There was a single large thick slice of\r\nbread by each boarder\xe2\x80\x99s plate, and it was observable that he economized\r\nit as if he were not expecting it to be duplicated. Dishes of butter\r\nwere distributed along the table within reach of people\xe2\x80\x99s arms, if they\r\nhad long ones, but there were no private butter plates. The butter was\r\nperhaps good enough, and was quiet and well behaved; but it had more\r\nbouquet than was necessary, though nobody commented upon that fact or\r\nseemed in any way disturbed by it. The main feature of the feast was a\r\npiping hot Irish stew made of the potatoes and meat left over from a\r\nprocession of previous meals. Everybody was liberally supplied with\r\nthis dish. On the table were a couple of great dishes of sliced ham,\r\nand there were some other eatables of minor importance\xe2\x80\x94preserves and\r\nNew Orleans molasses and such things. There was also plenty of tea and\r\ncoffee of an infernal sort, with brown sugar and condensed milk, but\r\nthe milk and sugar supply was not left at the discretion of the\r\nboarders, but was rationed out at headquarters\xe2\x80\x94one spoonful of sugar\r\nand one of condensed milk to each cup and no more. The table was waited\r\nupon by two stalwart negro women who raced back and forth from the\r\nbases of supplies with splendid dash and clatter and energy. Their\r\nlabors were supplemented after a fashion by the young girl Puss. She\r\ncarried coffee and tea back and forth among the boarders, but she made\r\npleasure excursions rather than business ones in this way, to speak\r\nstrictly. She made jokes with various people. She chaffed the young men\r\npleasantly and wittily, as she supposed, and as the rest also supposed,\r\napparently, judging by the applause and laughter which she got by her\r\nefforts. Manifestly she was a favorite with most of the young fellows\r\nand sweetheart of the rest of them. Where she conferred notice she\r\nconferred happiness, as was seen by the face of the recipient; and at\r\nthe same time she conferred unhappiness\xe2\x80\x94one could see it fall and dim\r\nthe faces of the other young fellows like a shadow. She never\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMistered\xe2\x80\x9d these friends of hers, but called them \xe2\x80\x9cBilly,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cTom,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn,\xe2\x80\x9d and they called her \xe2\x80\x9cPuss\xe2\x80\x9d or \xe2\x80\x9cHattie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Marsh sat at the head of the table, his wife sat at the foot. Marsh\r\nwas a man of sixty, and was an American; but if he had been born a\r\nmonth earlier he would have been a Spaniard. He was plenty good enough\r\nSpaniard as it was; his face was very dark, his hair very black, and\r\nhis eyes were not only exceedingly black but were very intense, and\r\nthere was something about them that indicated that they could burn with\r\npassion upon occasion. He was stoop-shouldered and lean-faced, and the\r\ngeneral aspect of him was disagreeable; he was evidently not a very\r\ncompanionable person. If looks went for anything, he was the very\r\nopposite of his wife, who was all motherliness and charity, good will\r\nand good nature. All the young men and the women called her Aunt\r\nRachael, which was another sign. Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s wandering and interested eye\r\npresently fell upon one boarder who had been overlooked in the\r\ndistribution of the stew. He was very pale and looked as if he had but\r\nlately come out of a sick bed, and also as if he ought to get back into\r\nit again as soon as possible. His face was very melancholy. The waves\r\nof laughter and conversation broke upon it without affecting it any\r\nmore than if it had been a rock in the sea and the words and the\r\nlaughter veritable waters. He held his head down and looked ashamed.\r\nSome of the women cast glances of pity toward him from time to time in\r\na furtive and half afraid way, and some of the youngest of the men\r\nplainly had compassion on the young fellow\xe2\x80\x94a compassion exhibited in\r\ntheir faces but not in any more active or compromising way. But the\r\ngreat majority of the people present showed entire indifference to the\r\nyouth and his sorrows. Marsh sat with his head down, but one could\r\ncatch the malicious gleam of his eyes through his shaggy brows. He was\r\nwatching that young fellow with evident relish. He had not neglected\r\nhim through carelessness, and apparently the table understood that\r\nfact. The spectacle was making Mrs. Marsh very uncomfortable. She had\r\nthe look of one who hopes against hope that the impossible may happen.\r\nBut as the impossible did not happen, she finally ventured to speak up\r\nand remind her husband that Nat Brady hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been helped to the Irish\r\nstew.\r\n\r\nMarsh lifted his head and gasped out with mock courtliness, \xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\r\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t, hasn\xe2\x80\x99t he? What a pity that is. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how I came to\r\noverlook him. Ah, he must pardon me. You must indeed\r\nMr\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94Baxter\xe2\x80\x94Barker, you must pardon me. I\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94my attention was directed\r\nto some other matter, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what. The thing that grieves me\r\nmainly is, that it happens every meal now. But you must try to overlook\r\nthese little things, Mr. Bunker, these little neglects on my part.\r\nThey\xe2\x80\x99re always likely to happen with me in any case, and they are\r\nespecially likely to happen where a person has\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94well, where a person\r\nis, say, about three weeks in arrears for his board. You get my\r\nmeaning?\xe2\x80\x94you get my idea? Here is your Irish stew, and\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94it gives me\r\nthe greatest pleasure to send it to you, and I hope that you will enjoy\r\nthe charity as much as I enjoy conferring it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA blush rose in Brady\xe2\x80\x99s white cheeks and flowed slowly backward to his\r\nears and upward toward his forehead, but he said nothing and began to\r\neat his food under the embarrassment of a general silence and the sense\r\nthat all eyes were fastened upon him. Barrow whispered to Tracy:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe old man\xe2\x80\x99s been waiting for that. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have missed that\r\nchance for anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a brutal business,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tracy. Then he said to himself,\r\npurposing to set the thought down in his diary later:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, here in this very house is a republic where all are free and\r\nequal, if men are free and equal anywhere in the earth, therefore I\r\nhave arrived at the place I started to find, and I am a man among men,\r\nand on the strictest equality possible to men, no doubt. Yet here on\r\nthe threshold I find an inequality. There are people at this table who\r\nare looked up to for some reason or another, and here is a poor devil\r\nof a boy who is looked down upon, treated with indifference, and shamed\r\nby humiliations, when he has committed no crime but that common one of\r\nbeing poor. Equality ought to make men noble-minded. In fact I had\r\nsupposed it did do that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter supper, Barrow proposed a walk, and they started. Barrow had a\r\npurpose. He wanted Tracy to get rid of that cowboy hat. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nhis way to finding mechanical or manual employment for a person rigged\r\nin that fashion. Barrow presently said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I understand it, you\xe2\x80\x99re not a cowboy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, now if you will not think me too curious, how did you come to\r\nmount that hat? Where\xe2\x80\x99d you get it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy didn\xe2\x80\x99t know quite how to reply to this, but presently said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, without going into particulars, I exchanged clothes with a\r\nstranger under stress of weather, and I would like to find him and\r\nre-exchange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you find him? Where is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I supposed the best way to find him would be to continue\r\nto wear his clothes, which are conspicuous enough to attract his\r\nattention if I should meet him on the street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Barrow, \xe2\x80\x9cthe rest of the outfit, is well enough,\r\nand while it\xe2\x80\x99s not too conspicuous, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t quite like the clothes\r\nthat anybody else wears. Suppress the hat. When you meet your man he\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nrecognize the rest of his suit. That\xe2\x80\x99s a mighty embarrassing hat, you\r\nknow, in a centre of civilization like this. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe an angel\r\ncould get employment in Washington in a halo like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np124.jpg (22K)\r\n\r\nTracy agreed to replace the hat with something of a modester form, and\r\nthey stepped aboard a crowded car and stood with others on the rear\r\nplatform. Presently, as the car moved swiftly along the rails, two men\r\ncrossing the street caught sight of the backs of Barrow and Tracy, and\r\nboth exclaimed at once, \xe2\x80\x9cThere he is!\xe2\x80\x9d It was Sellers and Hawkins. Both\r\nwere so paralyzed with joy that before they could pull themselves\r\ntogether and make an effort to stop the car, it was gone too far, and\r\nthey decided to wait for the next one. They waited a while; then it\r\noccurred to Washington that there could be no use in chasing one\r\nhorse-car with another, and he wanted to hunt up a hack. But the\r\nColonel said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you come to think of it, there\xe2\x80\x99s no occasion for that at all. Now\r\nthat I\xe2\x80\x99ve got him materialized, I can command his motions. I\xe2\x80\x99ll have\r\nhim at the house by the time we get there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen they hurried off home in a state of great and joyful excitement.\r\n\r\nThe hat exchange accomplished, the two new friends started to walk back\r\nleisurely to the boarding house. Barrow\xe2\x80\x99s mind was full of curiosity\r\nabout this young fellow. He said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve never been to the Rocky Mountains?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve never been out on the plains?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long have you been in this country?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly a few days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve never been in America before?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Barrow communed with himself. \xe2\x80\x9cNow what odd shapes the notions of\r\nromantic people take. Here\xe2\x80\x99s a young fellow who\xe2\x80\x99s read in England about\r\ncowboys and adventures on the plains. He comes here and buys a cowboy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsuit. Thinks he can play himself on folks for a cowboy, all\r\ninexperienced as he is. Now the minute he\xe2\x80\x99s caught in this poor little\r\ngame, he\xe2\x80\x99s ashamed of it and ready to retire from it. It is that\r\nexchange that he has put up as an explanation. It\xe2\x80\x99s rather thin, too\r\nthin altogether. Well, he\xe2\x80\x99s young, never been anywhere, knows nothing\r\nabout the world, sentimental, no doubt. Perhaps it was the natural\r\nthing for him to do, but it was a most singular choice, curious freak,\r\naltogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBoth men were busy with their thoughts for a time, then Tracy heaved a\r\nsigh and said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Barrow, the case of that young fellow troubles me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean Nat Brady?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Brady, or Baxter, or whatever it was. The old landlord called him\r\nby several different names.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, he has been very liberal with names for Brady, since Brady\r\nfell into arrears for his board. Well, that\xe2\x80\x99s one of his sarcasms\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nold man thinks he\xe2\x80\x99s great on sarcasm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what is Brady\xe2\x80\x99s difficulty? What is Brady\xe2\x80\x94who is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBrady is a tinner. He\xe2\x80\x99s a young journeyman tinner who was getting\r\nalong all right till he fell sick and lost his job. He was very popular\r\nbefore he lost his job; everybody in the house liked Brady. The old man\r\nwas rather especially fond of him, but you know that when a man loses\r\nhis job and loses his ability to support himself and to pay his way as\r\nhe goes, it makes a great difference in the way people look at him and\r\nfeel about him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that so! _Is_ it so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarrow looked at Tracy in a puzzled way. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy of course it\xe2\x80\x99s so.\r\nWouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you know that, naturally. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you know that the wounded deer\r\nis always attacked and killed by its companions and friends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy said to himself, while a chilly and boding discomfort spread\r\nitself through his system, \xe2\x80\x9cIn a republic of deer and men where all are\r\nfree and equal, misfortune is a crime, and the prosperous gore the\r\nunfortunate to death.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he said aloud, \xe2\x80\x9cHere in the boarding house,\r\nif one would have friends and be popular instead of having the cold\r\nshoulder turned upon him, he must be prosperous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Barrow said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is so. It\xe2\x80\x99s their human nature. They do turn\r\nagainst Brady, now that he\xe2\x80\x99s unfortunate, and they don\xe2\x80\x99t like him as\r\nwell as they did before; but it isn\xe2\x80\x99t because of any lack in Brady\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\njust as he was before, has the same nature and the same impulses, but\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x94well, Brady is a thorn in their consciences, you see. They know\r\nthey ought to help him and they\xe2\x80\x99re too stingy to do it, and they\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nashamed of themselves for that, and they ought also to hate themselves\r\non that account, but instead of that they hate Brady because he makes\r\nthem ashamed of themselves. I say that\xe2\x80\x99s human nature; that occurs\r\neverywhere; this boarding house is merely the world in little, it\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ncase all over\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re all alike. In prosperity we are popular;\r\npopularity comes easy in that case, but when the other thing comes our\r\nfriends are pretty likely to turn against us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy\xe2\x80\x99s noble theories and high purposes were beginning to feel pretty\r\ndamp and clammy. He wondered if by any possibility he had made a\r\nmistake in throwing his own prosperity to the winds and taking up the\r\ncross of other people\xe2\x80\x99s unprosperity. But he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t listen to that\r\nsort of thing; he cast it out of his mind and resolved to go ahead\r\nresolutely along the course he had mapped out for himself.\r\n\r\nExtracts from his diary:\r\n\r\n\r\nHave now spent several days in this singular hive. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know quite\r\nwhat to make out of these people. They have merits and virtues, but\r\nthey have some other qualities, and some ways that are hard to get\r\nalong with. I can\xe2\x80\x99t enjoy them. The moment I appeared in a hat of the\r\nperiod, I noticed a change. The respect which had been paid me before,\r\npassed suddenly away, and the people became friendly\xe2\x80\x94more than\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94they became familiar, and I\xe2\x80\x99m not used to familiarity, and can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ntake to it right off; I find that out. These people\xe2\x80\x99s familiarity\r\namounts to impudence, sometimes. I suppose it\xe2\x80\x99s all right; no doubt I\r\ncan get used to it, but it\xe2\x80\x99s not a satisfactory process at all. I have\r\naccomplished my dearest wish, I am a man among men, on an equal footing\r\nwith Tom, Dick and Harry, and yet it isn\xe2\x80\x99t just exactly what I thought\r\nit was going to be. I\xe2\x80\x94I miss home. Am obliged to say I am homesick.\r\nAnother thing\xe2\x80\x94and this is a confession\xe2\x80\x94a reluctant one, but I will make\r\nit: The thing I miss most and most severely, is the respect, the\r\ndeference, with which I was treated all my life in England, and which\r\nseems to be somehow necessary to me. I get along very well without the\r\nluxury and the wealth and the sort of society I\xe2\x80\x99ve been accustomed to,\r\nbut I do miss the respect and can\xe2\x80\x99t seem to get reconciled to the\r\nabsence of it. There is respect, there is deference here, but it\r\ndoesn\xe2\x80\x99t fall to my share. It is lavished on two men. One of them is a\r\nportly man of middle age who is a retired plumber. Everybody is pleased\r\nto have that man\xe2\x80\x99s notice. He\xe2\x80\x99s full of pomp and circumstance and self\r\ncomplacency and bad grammar, and at table he is Sir Oracle and when he\r\nopens his mouth not any dog in the kennel barks. The other person is a\r\npoliceman at the capitol-building. He represents the government. The\r\ndeference paid to these two men is not so very far short of that paid\r\nto an earl in England, though the method of it differs. Not so much\r\ncourtliness, but the deference is all there.\r\n\r\nYes, and there is obsequiousness, too.\r\n\r\nIt does rather look as if in a republic where all are free and equal,\r\nprosperity and position constitute _rank_.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe days drifted by, and they grew ever more dreary. For Barrow\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nefforts to find work for Tracy were unavailing. Always the first\r\nquestion asked was, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat Union do you belong to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy was obliged to reply that he didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to any trade-union.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, it\xe2\x80\x99s impossible to employ you. My men wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t stay\r\nwith me if I should employ a \xe2\x80\x98scab,\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98rat,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d or whatever the phrase\r\nwas.\r\n\r\nFinally, Tracy had a happy thought. He said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy the thing for me to\r\ndo, of course, is to _join_ a trade-union.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d Barrow said, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is the thing for you to do\xe2\x80\x94if you can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I _can?_ Is it difficult?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Yes,\xe2\x80\x9d Barrow said, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s sometimes difficult\xe2\x80\x94in fact, very\r\ndifficult. But you can try, and of course it will be best to try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTherefore Tracy tried; but he did not succeed. He was refused admission\r\nwith a good deal of promptness, and was advised to go back home, where\r\nhe belonged, not come here taking honest men\xe2\x80\x99s bread out of their\r\nmouths. Tracy began to realize that the situation was desperate, and\r\nthe thought made him cold to the marrow. He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cSo there\r\nis an aristocracy of position here, and an aristocracy of prosperity,\r\nand apparently there is also an aristocracy of the ins as opposed to\r\nthe outs, and I am with the outs. So the ranks grow daily, here.\r\nPlainly there are all kinds of castes here and only one that I belong\r\nto, the outcasts.\xe2\x80\x9d But he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t even smile at his small joke,\r\nalthough he was obliged to confess that he had a rather good opinion of\r\nit. He was feeling so defeated and miserable by this time that he could\r\nno longer look with philosophical complacency on the horseplay of the\r\nyoung fellows in the upper rooms at night. At first it had been\r\npleasant to see them unbend and have a good time after having so well\r\nearned it by the labors of the day, but now it all rasped upon his\r\nfeelings and his dignity. He lost patience with the spectacle. When\r\nthey were feeling good, they shouted, they scuffled, they sang songs,\r\nthey romped about the place like cattle, and they generally wound up\r\nwith a pillow fight, in which they banged each other over the head, and\r\nthrew the pillows in all directions, and every now and then he got a\r\nbuffet himself; and they were always inviting him to join in. They\r\ncalled him \xe2\x80\x9cJohnny Bull,\xe2\x80\x9d and invited him with excessive familiarity to\r\ntake a hand. At first he had endured all this with good nature, but\r\nlatterly he had shown by his manner that it was distinctly distasteful\r\nto him, and very soon he saw a change in the manner of these young\r\npeople toward him. They were souring on him as they would have\r\nexpressed it in their language. He had never been what might be called\r\npopular. That was hardly the phrase for it; he had merely been liked,\r\nbut now dislike for him was growing. His case was not helped by the\r\nfact that he was out of luck, couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get work, didn\xe2\x80\x99t belong to a\r\nunion, and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t gain admission to one. He got a good many slights\r\nof that small ill-defined sort that you can\xe2\x80\x99t quite put your finger on,\r\nand it was manifest that there was only one thing which protected him\r\nfrom open insult, and that was his muscle. These young people had seen\r\nhim exercising, mornings, after his cold sponge bath, and they had\r\nperceived by his performance and the build of his body, that he was\r\nathletic, and also versed in boxing. He felt pretty naked now,\r\nrecognizing that he was shorn of all respect except respect for his\r\nfists. One night when he entered his room he found about a dozen of the\r\nyoung fellows there carrying on a very lively conversation punctuated\r\nwith horse-laughter. The talking ceased instantly, and the frank\r\naffront of a dead silence followed. He said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood evening gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d and sat down.\r\n\r\nThere was no response. He flushed to the temples but forced himself to\r\nmaintain silence. He sat there in this uncomfortable stillness some\r\ntime, then got up and went out.\r\n\r\nThe moment he had disappeared he heard a prodigious shout of laughter\r\nbreak forth. He saw that their plain purpose had been to insult him. He\r\nascended to the flat roof, hoping to be able to cool down his spirit\r\nthere and get back his tranquility. He found the young tinner up there,\r\nalone and brooding, and entered into conversation with him. They were\r\npretty fairly matched, now, in unpopularity and general ill-luck and\r\nmisery, and they had no trouble in meeting upon this common ground with\r\nadvantage and something of comfort to both. But Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s movements had\r\nbeen watched, and in a few minutes the tormentors came straggling one\r\nafter another to the roof, where they began to stroll up and down in an\r\napparently purposeless way. But presently they fell to dropping remarks\r\nthat were evidently aimed at Tracy, and some of them at the tinner. The\r\nringleader of this little mob was a short-haired bully and amateur\r\nprize-fighter named Allen, who was accustomed to lording it over the\r\nupper floor, and had more than once shown a disposition to make trouble\r\nwith Tracy. Now there was an occasional cat-call, and hootings, and\r\nwhistlings, and finally the diversion of an exchange of connected\r\nremarks was introduced:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many does it take to make a pair?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, two generally makes a pair, but sometimes there ain\xe2\x80\x99t stuff\r\nenough in them to make a whole pair.\xe2\x80\x9d General laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat were you saying about the English a while ago?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nothing, the English are all right, only\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it you _said_ about them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I only said they swallow well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSwallow better than other people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, the English swallow a good deal better than other people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it they swallow best?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, insults.\xe2\x80\x9d Another general laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPretty hard to make \xe2\x80\x99em fight, ain\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, taint hard to make \xe2\x80\x99em fight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAin\xe2\x80\x99t it, really?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, taint hard. It\xe2\x80\x99s impossible.\xe2\x80\x9d Another laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis one\xe2\x80\x99s kind of spiritless, that\xe2\x80\x99s certain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ be the other way\xe2\x80\x94in his case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know the secret of his birth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo! has _he_ got a secret of his birth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou bet he has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis father was a wax-figger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAllen came strolling by where the pair were sitting; stopped, and said\r\nto the tinner;\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow are you off for friends, these days?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell enough off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot a good many?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, as many as I need.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA friend is valuable, sometimes\xe2\x80\x94as a protector, you know. What do you\r\nreckon would happen if I was to snatch your cap off and slap you in the\r\nface with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t trouble me, Mr. Allen, I ain\xe2\x80\x99t doing anything to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou answer me! What do you reckon would happen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy spoke up with a good deal of deliberation and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t trouble the young fellow, I can tell you what would happen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you can, can you? Boys, Johnny Bull can tell us what would happen\r\nif I was to snatch this chump\xe2\x80\x99s cap off and slap him in the face with\r\nit. Now you\xe2\x80\x99ll see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe snatched the cap and struck the youth in the face, and before he\r\ncould inquire what was going to happen, it had already happened, and he\r\nwas warming the tin with the broad of his back. Instantly there was a\r\nrush, and shouts of:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA ring, a ring, make a ring! Fair play all round! Johnny\xe2\x80\x99s grit; give\r\nhim a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe ring was quickly chalked on the tin, and Tracy found himself as\r\neager to begin as he could have been if his antagonist had been a\r\nprince instead of a mechanic. At bottom he was a little surprised at\r\nthis, because although his theories had been all in that direction for\r\nsome time, he was not prepared to find himself actually eager to\r\nmeasure strength with quite so common a man as this ruffian. In a\r\nmoment all the windows in the neighborhood were filled with people, and\r\nthe roofs also. The men squared off, and the fight began. But Allen\r\nstood no chance whatever, against the young Englishman. Neither in\r\nmuscle nor in science was he his equal. He measured his length on the\r\ntin time and again; in fact, as fast as he could get up he went down\r\nagain, and the applause was kept up in liberal fashion from all the\r\nneighborhood around. Finally, Allen had to be helped up. Then Tracy\r\ndeclined to punish him further and the fight was at an end. Allen was\r\ncarried off by some of his friends in a very much humbled condition,\r\nhis face black and blue and bleeding, and Tracy was at once surrounded\r\nby the young fellows, who congratulated him, and told him that he had\r\ndone the whole house a service, and that from this out Mr. Allen would\r\nbe a little more particular about how he handled slights and insults\r\nand maltreatment around amongst the boarders.\r\n\r\n\r\np135.jpg (28K)\r\n\r\nTracy was a hero now, and exceedingly popular. Perhaps nobody had ever\r\nbeen quite so popular on that upper floor before. But if being\r\ndiscountenanced by these young fellows had been hard to bear, their\r\nlavish commendations and approval and hero-worship were harder still to\r\nendure. He felt degraded, but he did not allow himself to analyze the\r\nreasons why, too closely. He was content to satisfy himself with the\r\nsuggestion that he looked upon himself as degraded by the public\r\nspectacle which he had made of himself, fighting on a tin roof, for the\r\ndelectation of everybody a block or two around. But he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t entirely\r\nsatisfied with that explanation of it. Once he went a little too far\r\nand wrote in his diary that his case was worse than that of the\r\nprodigal son. He said the prodigal son merely fed swine, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nto chum with them. But he struck that out, and said \xe2\x80\x9cAll men are equal.\r\nI will not disown my principles. These men are as good as I am.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy was become popular on the lower floors also. Everybody was\r\ngrateful for Allen\xe2\x80\x99s reduction to the ranks, and for his transformation\r\nfrom a doer of outrages to a mere threatener of them. The young girls,\r\nof whom there were half a dozen, showed many attentions to Tracy,\r\nparticularly that boarding house pet Hattie, the landlady\xe2\x80\x99s daughter.\r\nShe said to him, very sweetly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99re ever so nice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd when he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad you think so, Miss Hattie,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, still\r\nmore sweetly,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t call me Miss Hattie\xe2\x80\x94call me Puss.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAh, here was promotion! He had struck the summit. There were no higher\r\nheights to climb in that boarding house. His popularity was complete.\r\n\r\nIn the presence of people, Tracy showed a tranquil outside, but his\r\nheart was being eaten out of him by distress and despair.\r\n\r\nIn a little while he should be out of money, and then what should he\r\ndo? He wished, now, that he had borrowed a little more liberally from\r\nthat stranger\xe2\x80\x99s store. He found it impossible to sleep. A single\r\ntorturing, terrifying thought went racking round and round in his head,\r\nwearing a groove in his brain: What should he do\xe2\x80\x94What was to become of\r\nhim? And along with it began to intrude a something presently which was\r\nvery like a wish that he had not joined the great and noble ranks of\r\nmartyrdom, but had stayed at home and been content to be merely an earl\r\nand nothing better, with nothing more to do in this world of a useful\r\nsort than an earl finds to do. But he smothered that part of his\r\nthought as well as he could; he made every effort to drive it away, and\r\nwith fair success, but he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t keep it from intruding a little now\r\nand then, and when it intruded it came suddenly and nipped him like a\r\nbite, a sting, a burn. He recognized that thought by the peculiar\r\nsharpness of its pang. The others were painful enough, but that one cut\r\nto the quick when it came. Night after night he lay tossing to the\r\nmusic of the hideous snoring of the honest bread-winners until two and\r\nthree o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning, then got up and took refuge on the roof,\r\nwhere he sometimes got a nap and sometimes failed entirely. His\r\nappetite was leaving him and the zest of life was going along with it.\r\nFinally, one day, being near the imminent verge of total\r\ndiscouragement, he said to himself\xe2\x80\x94and took occasion to blush privately\r\nwhen he said it, \xe2\x80\x9cIf my father knew what my American name is,\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x94well,\r\nmy duty to my father rather _requires_ that I furnish him my name. I\r\nhave no right to make his days and nights unhappy, I can do enough\r\nunhappiness for the family all by myself. Really he ought to know what\r\nmy American name is.\xe2\x80\x9d He thought over it a while and framed a cablegram\r\nin his mind to this effect:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy American name is Howard Tracy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be suggesting anything. His father could understand that\r\nas he chose, and doubtless he would understand it as it was meant, as a\r\ndutiful and affectionate desire on the part of a son to make his old\r\nfather happy for a moment. Continuing his train of thought, Tracy said\r\nto himself, \xe2\x80\x9cAh, but if he should cable me to come home! I\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndo that\xe2\x80\x94I _mustn\xe2\x80\x99t_ do that. I\xe2\x80\x99ve started out on a mission, and I\r\nmustn\xe2\x80\x99t turn my back on it in cowardice. No, no, I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t go home,\r\nat\xe2\x80\x94at\xe2\x80\x94least I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want to go home.\xe2\x80\x9d After a reflective pause:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, maybe\xe2\x80\x94perhaps\xe2\x80\x94it would be my _duty_ to go in the circumstances;\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99s very old and he does need me by him to stay his footsteps down the\r\nlong hill that inclines westward toward the sunset of his life. Well,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll think about that. Yes, of course it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be right to stay\r\nhere. If I\xe2\x80\x94well, perhaps I could just drop him a line and put it off a\r\nlittle while and satisfy him in that way. It would be\xe2\x80\x94well, it would\r\nmar everything to have him require me to come instantly.\xe2\x80\x9d Another\r\nreflective pause\xe2\x80\x94then: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet if he should do that I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nbut\xe2\x80\x94oh, dear me\xe2\x80\x94_home!_ how good it sounds! and a body is excusable for\r\nwanting to see his home again, now and then, anyway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went to one of the telegraph offices in the avenue and got the first\r\nend of what Barrow called the \xe2\x80\x9cusual Washington courtesy,\xe2\x80\x9d where \xe2\x80\x9cthey\r\ntreat you as a tramp until they find out you\xe2\x80\x99re a congressman, and then\r\nthey slobber all over you.\xe2\x80\x9d There was a boy of seventeen on duty there,\r\ntying his shoe. He had his foot on a chair and his back turned toward\r\nthe wicket. He glanced over his shoulder, took Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s measure, turned\r\nback, and went on tying his shoe. Tracy finished writing his telegram\r\nand waited, still waited, and still waited, for that performance to\r\nfinish, but there didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to be any finish to it; so finally Tracy\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you take my telegram?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe youth looked over his shoulder and said, by his manner, not his\r\nwords:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think you could wait a minute, if you tried?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHowever, he got the shoe tied at last, and came and took the telegram,\r\nglanced over it, then looked up surprised, at Tracy. There was\r\nsomething in his look that bordered upon respect, almost reverence, it\r\nseemed to Tracy, although he had been so long without anything of this\r\nkind he was not sure that he knew the signs of it.\r\n\r\nThe boy read the address aloud, with pleased expression in face and\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Earl of Rossmore! Cracky! Do you know him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that so! Does he know you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I swear! Will he answer you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think he will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill he though? Where\xe2\x80\x99ll you have it sent?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, nowhere. I\xe2\x80\x99ll call here and get it. When shall I call?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll send it to you. Where shall I send it? Give me\r\nyour address; I\xe2\x80\x99ll send it to you soon\xe2\x80\x99s it comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Tracy didn\xe2\x80\x99t propose to do this. He had acquired the boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nadmiration and deferential respect, and he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t willing to throw\r\nthese precious things away, a result sure to follow if he should give\r\nthe address of that boarding house. So he said again that he would call\r\nand get the telegram, and went his way.\r\n\r\nHe idled along, reflecting. He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cThere _is_ something\r\npleasant about being respected. I have acquired the respect of Mr.\r\nAllen and some of those others, and almost the deference of some of\r\nthem on pure merit, for having thrashed Allen. While their respect and\r\ntheir deference\xe2\x80\x94if it is deference\xe2\x80\x94is pleasant, a deference based upon\r\na sham, a shadow, does really seem pleasanter still. It\xe2\x80\x99s no real merit\r\nto be in correspondence with an earl, and yet after all, that boy makes\r\nme feel as if there was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cablegram was actually gone home! the thought of it gave him an\r\nimmense uplift. He walked with a lighter tread. His heart was full of\r\nhappiness. He threw aside all hesitances and confessed to himself that\r\nhe was glad through and through that he was going to give up this\r\nexperiment and go back to his home again. His eagerness to get his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s answer began to grow, now, and it grew with marvelous\r\ncelerity, after it began. He waited an hour, walking about, putting in\r\nhis time as well as he could, but interested in nothing that came under\r\nhis eye, and at last he presented himself at the office again and asked\r\nif any answer had come yet. The boy said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no answer yet,\xe2\x80\x9d then glanced at the clock and added, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink it\xe2\x80\x99s likely you\xe2\x80\x99ll get one to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you see it\xe2\x80\x99s getting pretty late. You can\xe2\x80\x99t always tell where\r\n\xe2\x80\x99bouts a man is when he\xe2\x80\x99s on the other side, and you can\xe2\x80\x99t always find\r\nhim just the minute you want him, and you see it\xe2\x80\x99s getting about six\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock now, and over there it\xe2\x80\x99s pretty late at night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tracy, \xe2\x80\x9cI hadn\xe2\x80\x99t thought of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, pretty late, now, half past ten or eleven. Oh yes, you probably\r\nwon\xe2\x80\x99t get any answer to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV.\r\n\r\n\r\nSo Tracy went home to supper. The odors in that supper room seemed more\r\nstrenuous and more horrible than ever before, and he was happy in the\r\nthought that he was so soon to be free from them again. When the supper\r\nwas over he hardly knew whether he had eaten any of it or not, and he\r\ncertainly hadn\xe2\x80\x99t heard any of the conversation. His heart had been\r\ndancing all the time, his thoughts had been faraway from these things,\r\nand in the visions of his mind the sumptuous appointments of his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s castle had risen before him without rebuke. Even the plushed\r\nflunkey, that walking symbol of a sham inequality, had not been\r\nunpleasant to his dreaming view. After the meal Barrow said,\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome with me. I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you a jolly evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good. Where are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo my club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat club is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMechanics\xe2\x80\x99 Debating Club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np144.jpg (33K)\r\n\r\nTracy shuddered, slightly. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t say anything about having visited\r\nthat place himself. Somehow he didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite relish the memory of that\r\ntime. The sentiments which had made his former visit there so\r\nenjoyable, and filled him with such enthusiasm, had undergone a gradual\r\nchange, and they had rotted away to such a degree that he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ncontemplate another visit there with anything strongly resembling\r\ndelight. In fact he was a little ashamed to go; he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to go\r\nthere and find out by the rude impact of the thought of those people\r\nupon his reorganized condition of mind, how sharp the change had been.\r\nHe would have preferred to stay away. He expected that now he should\r\nhear nothing except sentiments which would be a reproach to him in his\r\nchanged mental attitude, and he rather wished he might be excused. And\r\nyet he didn\xe2\x80\x99t quite want to say that, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to show how he did\r\nfeel, or show any disinclination to go, and so he forced himself to go\r\nalong with Barrow, privately purposing to take an early opportunity to\r\nget away.\r\n\r\nAfter the essayist of the evening had read his paper, the chairman\r\nannounced that the debate would now be upon the subject of the previous\r\nmeeting, \xe2\x80\x9cThe American Press.\xe2\x80\x9d It saddened the backsliding disciple to\r\nhear this announcement. It brought up too many reminiscences. He wished\r\nhe had happened upon some other subject. But the debate began, and he\r\nsat still and listened.\r\n\r\nIn the course of the discussion one of the speakers\xe2\x80\x94a blacksmith named\r\nTompkins arraigned all monarchs and all lords in the earth for their\r\ncold selfishness in retaining their unearned dignities. He said that no\r\nmonarch and no son of a monarch, no lord and no son of a lord ought to\r\nbe able to look his fellow man in the face without shame. Shame for\r\nconsenting to keep his unearned titles, property, and privileges\xe2\x80\x94at the\r\nexpense of other people; shame for consenting to remain, on any terms,\r\nin dishonourable possession of these things, which represented bygone\r\nrobberies and wrongs inflicted upon the general people of the nation.\r\nHe said, \xe2\x80\x9cif there were a lord or the son of a lord here, I would like\r\nto reason with him, and try to show him how unfair and how selfish his\r\nposition is. I would try to persuade him to relinquish it, take his\r\nplace among men on equal terms, earn the bread he eats, and hold of\r\nslight value all deference paid him because of artificial position, all\r\nreverence not the just due of his own personal merits.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy seemed to be listening to utterances of his own made in talks\r\nwith his radical friends in England. It was as if some eavesdropping\r\nphonograph had treasured up his words and brought them across the\r\nAtlantic to accuse him with them in the hour of his defection and\r\nretreat. Every word spoken by this stranger seemed to leave a blister\r\non Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s conscience, and by the time the speech was finished he felt\r\nthat he was all conscience and one blister. This man\xe2\x80\x99s deep compassion\r\nfor the enslaved and oppressed millions in Europe who had to bear with\r\nthe contempt of that small class above them, throned upon shining\r\nheights whose paths were shut against them, was the very thing he had\r\noften uttered himself. The pity in this man\xe2\x80\x99s voice and words was the\r\nvery twin of the pity that used to reside in his own heart and come\r\nfrom his own lips when he thought of these oppressed peoples.\r\n\r\nThe homeward tramp was accomplished in brooding silence. It was a\r\nsilence most grateful to Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s feelings. He wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have broken it\r\nfor anything; for he was ashamed of himself all the way through to his\r\nspine. He kept saying to himself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow unanswerable it all is\xe2\x80\x94how absolutely unanswerable! It is basely,\r\ndegradingly selfish to keep those unearned honors, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94oh, hang it,\r\nnobody but a cur\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat an idiotic damned speech that Tompkins made!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis outburst was from Barrow. It flooded Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s demoralized soul with\r\nwaters of refreshment. These were the darlingest words the poor\r\nvacillating young apostate had ever heard\xe2\x80\x94for they whitewashed his\r\nshame for him, and that is a good service to have when you can\xe2\x80\x99t get\r\nthe best of all verdicts, self-acquittal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome up to my room and smoke a pipe, Tracy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy had been expecting this invitation, and had had his declination\r\nall ready: but he was glad enough to accept, now. Was it possible that\r\na reasonable argument could be made against that man\xe2\x80\x99s desolating\r\nspeech? He was burning to hear Barrow try it. He knew how to start him,\r\nand keep him going: it was to seem to combat his positions\xe2\x80\x94a process\r\neffective with most people.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you object to in Tompkins\xe2\x80\x99s speech, Barrow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the leaving out of the factor of human nature; requiring another\r\nman to do what you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy here\xe2\x80\x99s what I mean; it\xe2\x80\x99s very simple. Tompkins is a blacksmith;\r\nhas a family; works for wages; and hard, too\xe2\x80\x94fooling around won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfurnish the bread. Suppose it should turn out that by the death of\r\nsomebody in England he is suddenly an earl\xe2\x80\x94income, half a million\r\ndollars a year. What would he do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x94I suppose he would have to decline to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan, he would grab it in a second!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think he would?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink?\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t think anything about it, I know it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? Because he\xe2\x80\x99s not a fool.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you think that if he were a fool, he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t. Fool or _no_ fool, he would grab it. Anybody would.\r\nAnybody that\xe2\x80\x99s alive. And I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen dead people that would get up and\r\ngo for it. I would myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np148.jpg (24K)\r\n\r\nThis was balm, this was healing, this was rest and peace and comfort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought you were opposed to nobilities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTransmissible ones, yes. But that\xe2\x80\x99s nothing. I\xe2\x80\x99m opposed to\r\nmillionaires, but it would be dangerous to offer me the position.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99d take it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would leave the funeral of my dearest enemy to go and assume its\r\nburdens and responsibilities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy thought a while, then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I quite get the bearings of your position. You say\r\nyou are opposed to hereditary nobilities, and yet if you had the chance\r\nyou would\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake one? In a minute I would. And there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a mechanic in that\r\nentire club that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t a lawyer, doctor, editor,\r\nauthor, tinker, loafer, railroad president, saint\xe2\x80\x94land, there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a\r\nhuman _being_ in the United States that wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t jump at the chance!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tracy softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept you!\xe2\x80\x9d Barrow could hardly get the words out, his scorn so\r\nchoked him. And he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get any further than that form of words; it\r\nseemed to dam his flow, utterly. He got up and came and glared upon\r\nTracy in a kind of outraged and unappeasable way, and said again,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcept _you!_\xe2\x80\x9d He walked around him\xe2\x80\x94inspecting him from one point of\r\nview and then another, and relieving his soul now and then by exploding\r\nthat formula at him; \xe2\x80\x9cExcept _you!_\xe2\x80\x9d Finally he slumped down into his\r\nchair with the air of one who gives it up, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s straining his viscera and he\xe2\x80\x99s breaking his heart trying to get\r\nsome low-down job that a good dog wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have, and yet wants to let\r\non that if he had a chance to scoop an earldom he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do it.\r\nTracy, don\xe2\x80\x99t put this kind of a strain on me. Lately I\xe2\x80\x99m not as strong\r\nas I was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t meaning to put\xe2\x80\x94a strain on you, Barrow, I was only\r\nmeaning to intimate that if an earldom ever does fall in my way\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x94I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give myself any worry about _that_, if I was you. Are you any different from\r\nme?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you any better than me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO,\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94why, certainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you as _good?_ Come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, I\xe2\x80\x94the fact is you take me so suddenly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuddenly? What is there sudden about it? It isn\xe2\x80\x99t a difficult question\r\nis it? Or doubtful? Just measure us on the only fair lines\xe2\x80\x94the lines of\r\nmerit\xe2\x80\x94and of course you\xe2\x80\x99ll admit that a journeyman chairmaker that\r\nearns his twenty dollars a week, and has had the good and genuine\r\nculture of contact with men, and care, and hardship, and failure, and\r\nsuccess, and downs and ups and ups and downs, is just a trifle the\r\nsuperior of a young fellow like you, who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know how to do\r\nanything that\xe2\x80\x99s valuable, can\xe2\x80\x99t earn his living in any secure and\r\nsteady way, hasn\xe2\x80\x99t had any experience of life and its seriousness,\r\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t any culture but the artificial culture of books, which adorns\r\nbut doesn\xe2\x80\x99t really educate\xe2\x80\x94come! if _I_ wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t scorn an earldom, what\r\nthe devil right have _you_ to do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy dissembled his joy, though he wanted to thank the chair-maker for\r\nthat last remark. Presently a thought struck him, and he spoke up\r\nbriskly and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut look here, I really can\xe2\x80\x99t quite get the hang of your notions\xe2\x80\x94your\r\nprinciples, if they are principles. You are inconsistent. You are\r\nopposed to aristocracies, yet you\xe2\x80\x99d take an earldom if you could. I am entirely clear-headed about this thing. If I\r\ncould extirpate an aristocratic system by declining its honors, _then_\r\nI should be a rascal to accept them. And if enough of the mass would\r\njoin me to make the extirpation possible, _then_ I should be a rascal\r\nto do otherwise than help in the attempt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe I understand\xe2\x80\x94yes, I think I get the idea. You have no blame\r\nfor the lucky few who naturally decline to vacate the pleasant nest\r\nthey were born into, you only despise the all-powerful and stupid mass\r\nof the nation for allowing the nest to exist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it, that\xe2\x80\x99s it! You _can_ get a simple thing through your head\r\nif you work at it long enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t mention it. I begin to think you\xe2\x80\x99ve got a good deal of\r\nimagination. With you, the idlest fancy freezes into a reality at a\r\nbreath. Why, you looked, then, as if it wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t astonish you if you\r\ndid tumble into an earldom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy blushed. Barrow added: \xe2\x80\x9cEarldom! Oh, yes, take it, if it offers;\r\nbut meantime we\xe2\x80\x99ll go on looking around, in a modest way, and if you\r\nget a chance to superintend a sausage-stuffer at six or eight dollars a\r\nweek, you just trade off the earldom for a last year\xe2\x80\x99s almanac and\r\nstick to the sausage-stuffing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV.\r\n\r\n\r\nTracy went to bed happy once more, at rest in his mind once more. He\r\nhad started out on a high emprise\xe2\x80\x94that was to his credit, he argued; he\r\nhad fought the best fight he could, considering the odds against\r\nhim\xe2\x80\x94that was to his credit; he had been defeated\xe2\x80\x94certainly there was\r\nnothing discreditable in that. Being defeated, he had a right to retire\r\nwith the honors of war and go back without prejudice to the position in\r\nthe world\xe2\x80\x99s society to which he had been born. Why not? even the rabid\r\nrepublican chair-maker would do that. Yes, his conscience was\r\ncomfortable once more.\r\n\r\nHe woke refreshed, happy, and eager for his cablegram. He had been born\r\nan aristocrat, he had been a democrat for a time, he was now an\r\naristocrat again. He marveled to find that this final change was not\r\nmerely intellectual, it had invaded his feeling; and he also marveled\r\nto note that this feeling seemed a good deal less artificial than any\r\nhe had entertained in his system for a long time. He could also have\r\nnoted, if he had thought of it, that his bearing had stiffened, over\r\nnight, and that his chin had lifted itself a shade. Arrived in the\r\nbasement, he was about to enter the breakfast room when he saw old\r\nMarsh in the dim light of a corner of the hall, beckoning him with his\r\nfinger to approach. The blood welled slowly up in Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s cheek, and he\r\nsaid with a grade of injured dignity almost ducal:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the purpose of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to speak to you\xe2\x80\x94in private.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis spot is private enough for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMarsh was surprised; and not particularly pleased. He approached and\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, in public, then, if you prefer. Though it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been my way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boarders gathered to the spot, interested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak out,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tracy. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it you want?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94forgot something?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? I\xe2\x80\x99m not aware of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re not? Now you stop and think, a minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI refuse to stop and think. It doesn\xe2\x80\x99t interest me. If it interests\r\nyou, speak out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marsh, raising his voice to a slightly angry pitch,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou forgot to pay your board yesterday\xe2\x80\x94if you\xe2\x80\x99re _bound_ to have it\r\npublic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOh, yes, this heir to an annual million or so had been dreaming and\r\nsoaring, and had forgotten that pitiful three or four dollars. For\r\npenalty he must have it coarsely flung in his face in the presence of\r\nthese people\xe2\x80\x94people in whose countenances was already beginning to dawn\r\nan uncharitable enjoyment of the situation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs _that_ all! Take your money and give your terrors a rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy\xe2\x80\x99s hand went down into his pocket with angry decision. But\xe2\x80\x94it\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t come out. The color began to ebb out of his face. The\r\ncountenances about him showed a growing interest; and some of them a\r\nheightened satisfaction. It\xe2\x80\x99s too old\xe2\x80\x94been played in this\r\nhouse too often; everybody plays it that can\xe2\x80\x99t get work when he wants\r\nit, and won\xe2\x80\x99t work when he can get it. Trot out Mr. Allen, somebody,\r\nand let _him_ take a toot at it. It\xe2\x80\x99s _his_ turn next, _he_ forgot,\r\ntoo, last night. I\xe2\x80\x99m laying for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne of the negro women came scrambling down stairs as pale as a sorrel\r\nhorse with consternation and excitement:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMisto Marsh, Misto Allen\xe2\x80\x99s skipped out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes-sah, and cleaned out his room _clean;_ tuck bofe towels en de\r\nsoap!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou lie, you hussy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s jes\xe2\x80\x99 so, jes\xe2\x80\x99 as I tells you\xe2\x80\x94en Misto Summer\xe2\x80\x99s socks is gone, en\r\nMisto Naylor\xe2\x80\x99s yuther shirt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Marsh was at boiling point by this time. He turned upon Tracy:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer up now\xe2\x80\x94when are you going to settle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-day\xe2\x80\x94since you seem to be in a hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_To-day_ is it? Sunday\xe2\x80\x94and you out of work? I like that. Come\xe2\x80\x94where\r\nare you going to get the money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy\xe2\x80\x99s spirit was rising again. He proposed to impress these people:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am expecting a cablegram from home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOld Marsh was caught out, with the surprise of it. The idea was so\r\nimmense, so extravagant, that he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get his breath at first. When\r\nhe did get it, it came rancid with sarcasm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA _cablegram_\xe2\x80\x94think of it, ladies and gents, he\xe2\x80\x99s expecting a\r\ncablegram! _He\xe2\x80\x99s_ expecting a cablegram\xe2\x80\x94this duffer, this scrub, this\r\nbilk! From his father\xe2\x80\x94eh? Yes\xe2\x80\x94without a doubt. A dollar or two a\r\nword\xe2\x80\x94oh, that\xe2\x80\x99s nothing\xe2\x80\x94_they_ don\xe2\x80\x99t mind a little thing like\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94_this_ kind\xe2\x80\x99s fathers don\xe2\x80\x99t. Tracy was too angry to realize that he had done a foolish\r\nthing. He said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStand aside, please. I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute, your lordship,\xe2\x80\x9d said Marsh, bowing low, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere is your\r\nlordship going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor the cablegram. It means\r\nthat I am not the kind that can be taken in by every hack-driver\xe2\x80\x99s son\r\nthat comes loafing over here because he can\xe2\x80\x99t bum a living at home. It\r\nmeans that you can\xe2\x80\x99t skip out on any such\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTracy made a step toward the old man, but Mrs. Marsh sprang between,\r\nand said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t, Mr. Tracy, please.\xe2\x80\x9d She turned to her husband and said, \xe2\x80\x9c_Do_\r\nbridle your tongue. What has he done to be treated so? Can\xe2\x80\x99t you _see_\r\nhe has lost his mind, with trouble and distress? He\xe2\x80\x99s not responsible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank your kind heart, madam, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve not lost my mind; and if I can\r\nhave the mere privilege of stepping to the telegraph office\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you can\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Marsh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94or sending\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSending! If there\xe2\x80\x99s anybody that\xe2\x80\x99s fool enough\r\nto go on such a chuckle-headed errand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere comes Mr. Barrow\xe2\x80\x94he will go for me. Barrow\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA brisk fire of exclamations broke out\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay, Barrow, he\xe2\x80\x99s expecting a cablegram!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCablegram from his father, you know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94cablegram from the wax-figger!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd say, Barrow, this fellow\xe2\x80\x99s an earl\xe2\x80\x94take off your hat, pull down\r\nyour vest!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, he\xe2\x80\x99s come off and forgot his crown, that he wears Sundays. He\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncabled over to his pappy to send it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou step out and get that cablegram, Barrow; his majesty\xe2\x80\x99s a little\r\nlame to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh stop,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Barrow; \xe2\x80\x9cgive the man a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned, and said\r\nwith some severity, \xe2\x80\x9cTracy, what\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with you?
