I\xe2\x80\x99m your friend in trouble and out of it, before\r\nyour face and behind your back, for anything in _reason;_ but you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nlost your head, you see, and this moonshine about a cablegram\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I\xe2\x80\x99ll_ go there and ask for it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you from the bottom of my heart, Brady. Here, I\xe2\x80\x99ll give you a\r\nWritten order for it. Fly, now, and fetch it. We\xe2\x80\x99ll soon see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBrady flew. Immediately the sort of quiet began to steal over the crowd\r\nwhich means dawning doubt, misgiving; and might be translated into the\r\nwords, \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe he _is_ expecting a cablegram\xe2\x80\x94maybe he _has_ got a father\r\nsomewhere\xe2\x80\x94maybe we\xe2\x80\x99ve been just a little too fresh, just a shade too\r\n\xe2\x80\x98previous\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLoud talk ceased; then the mutterings and low murmurings and\r\nwhisperings died out. The crowd began to crumble apart. By ones and\r\ntwos the fragments drifted to the breakfast table. Barrow tried to\r\nbring Tracy in; but he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yet, Barrow\xe2\x80\x94presently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Marsh and Hattie tried, offering gentle and kindly persuasions;\r\nbut he said;\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would rather wait\xe2\x80\x94till he comes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEven old Marsh began to have suspicions that maybe he had been a trifle\r\ntoo \xe2\x80\x9cbrash,\xe2\x80\x9d as he called it in the privacy of his soul, and he pulled\r\nhimself together and started toward Tracy with invitation in his eyes;\r\nbut Tracy warned him off with a gesture which was quite positive and\r\neloquent. Then followed the stillest quarter of an hour which had ever\r\nbeen known in that house at that time of day. It was so still, and so\r\nsolemn withal, that when somebody\xe2\x80\x99s cup slipped from his fingers and\r\nlanded in his plate the shock made people start, and the sharp sound\r\nseemed as indecorous there and as out of place as if a coffin and\r\nmourners were imminent and being waited for. And at last when Brady\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfeet came clattering down the stairs the sacrilege seemed unbearable.\r\nEverybody rose softly and turned toward the door, where stood Tracy;\r\nthen with a common impulse, moved a step or two in that direction, and\r\nstopped. While they gazed, young Brady arrived, panting, and put into\r\nTracy\xe2\x80\x99s hand,\xe2\x80\x94sure enough\xe2\x80\x94an envelope. Tracy fastened a bland\r\nvictorious eye upon the gazers, and kept it there till one by one they\r\ndropped their eyes, vanquished and embarrassed. Then he tore open the\r\ntelegram and glanced at its message. The yellow paper fell from his\r\nfingers and fluttered to the floor, and his face turned white. There\r\nwas nothing there but one word\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Thanks_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe humorist of the house, the tall, raw-boned Billy Nash, caulker from\r\nthe navy yard, was standing in the rear of the crowd. In the midst of\r\nthe pathetic silence that was now brooding over the place and moving\r\nsome few hearts there toward compassion, he began to whimper, then he\r\nput his handkerchief to his eyes and buried his face in the neck of the\r\nbashfulest young fellow in the company, a navy-yard blacksmith,\r\nshrieked \xe2\x80\x9cOh, pappy, how _could_ you!\xe2\x80\x9d and began to bawl like a\r\nteething baby, if one may imagine a baby with the energy and the\r\ndevastating voice of a jackass.\r\n\r\nSo perfect was that imitation of a child\xe2\x80\x99s cry, and so vast the scale\r\nof it and so ridiculous the aspect of the performer, that all gravity\r\nwas swept from the place as if by a hurricane, and almost everybody\r\nthere joined in the crash of laughter provoked by the exhibition. Then\r\nthe small mob began to take its revenge\xe2\x80\x94revenge for the discomfort and\r\napprehension it had brought upon itself by its own too rash freshness\r\nof a little while before. It guyed its poor victim, baited him, worried\r\nhim, as dogs do with a cornered cat. The victim answered back with\r\ndefiances and challenges which included everybody, and which only gave\r\nthe sport new spirit and variety; but when he changed his tactics and\r\nbegan to single out individuals and invite them by name, the fun lost\r\nits funniness and the interest of the show died out, along with the\r\nnoise.\r\n\r\nFinally Marsh was about to take an innings, but Barrow said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, now\xe2\x80\x94leave him alone. You\xe2\x80\x99ve no account with him but a\r\nmoney account. I\xe2\x80\x99ll take care of that myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe distressed and worried landlady gave Barrow a fervently grateful\r\nlook for his championship of the abused stranger; and the pet of the\r\nhouse, a very prism in her cheap but ravishing Sunday rig, blew him a\r\nkiss from the tips of her fingers and said, with the darlingest smile\r\nand a sweet little toss of her head:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re the only man here, and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to set my cap for you, you\r\ndear old thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame, Puss! How you talk! I _never_ saw such a child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt took a good deal of argument and persuasion\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, petting,\r\nunder these disguises\xe2\x80\x94to get Tracy to entertain the idea of breakfast.\r\nHe at first said he would never eat again in that house; and added that\r\nhe had enough firmness of character, he trusted, to enable him to\r\nstarve like a man when the alternative was to eat insult with his\r\nbread.\r\n\r\nWhen he had finished his breakfast, Barrow took him to his room,\r\nfurnished him a pipe, and said cheerily:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Now_, old fellow, take in your battle-flag out of the wet, you\xe2\x80\x99re not\r\nin the hostile camp any more. You\xe2\x80\x99re a little upset by your troubles,\r\nand that\xe2\x80\x99s natural enough, but don\xe2\x80\x99t let your mind run on them anymore\r\nthan you can help; drag your thoughts away from your troubles by the\r\nears, by the heels, or any other way, so you manage it; it\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nhealthiest thing a body can do; dwelling on troubles is deadly, just\r\ndeadly\xe2\x80\x94and that\xe2\x80\x99s the softest name there is for it. You must keep your\r\nmind amused\xe2\x80\x94you must, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, miserable me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Don\xe2\x80\x99t!_ There\xe2\x80\x99s just pure heart-break in that tone. It\xe2\x80\x99s just as I\r\nsay; you\xe2\x80\x99ve got to get right down to it and amuse your mind, as if it\r\nwas salvation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re easy words to say, Barrow, but how am I going to amuse,\r\nentertain, divert a mind that finds itself suddenly assaulted and\r\noverwhelmed by disasters of a sort not dreamed of and not provided for?\r\nNo\xe2\x80\x94no, the bare idea of amusement is repulsive to my feelings: Let us\r\ntalk of death and funerals.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94not yet. That would be giving up the ship. We\xe2\x80\x99ll not give up the\r\nship yet. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to amuse you; I sent Brady out for the wherewithal\r\nbefore you finished breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did? What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, this is a good sign\xe2\x80\x94curiosity. Oh, there\xe2\x80\x99s hope for you yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI.\r\n\r\n\r\nBrady arrived with a box, and departed, after saying, \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re\r\nfinishing one up, but they\xe2\x80\x99ll be along as soon as it\xe2\x80\x99s done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBarrow took a frameless oil portrait a foot square from the box, set it\r\nup in a good light, without comment, and reached for another, taking a\r\nfurtive glance at Tracy, meantime. The stony solemnity in Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\nremained as it was, and gave out no sign of interest. Barrow placed the\r\nsecond portrait beside the first, and stole another glance while\r\nreaching for a third. The stone image softened, a shade. No. 3 forced\r\nthe ghost of a smile, No. 4 swept indifference wholly away, and No. 5\r\nstarted a laugh which was still in good and hearty condition when No.\r\n14 took its place in the row.\r\n\r\n\r\np162.jpg (17K)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _you\xe2\x80\x99re_ all right, yet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Barrow. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see you\xe2\x80\x99re not past\r\namusement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe pictures were fearful, as to color, and atrocious as to drawing and\r\nexpression; but the feature which squelched animosity and made them\r\nfunny was a feature which could not achieve its full force in a single\r\npicture, but required the wonder-working assistance of repetition. One\r\nloudly dressed mechanic in stately attitude, with his hand on a cannon,\r\nashore, and a ship riding at anchor in the offing,\xe2\x80\x94this is merely odd;\r\nbut when one sees the same cannon and the same ship in fourteen\r\npictures in a row, and a different mechanic standing watch in each, the\r\nthing gets to be funny.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain\xe2\x80\x94explain these aberrations,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tracy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, they are not the achievement of a single intellect, a single\r\ntalent\xe2\x80\x94it takes two to do these miracles. They are collaborations; the\r\none artist does the figure, the other the accessories. The\r\nfigure-artist is a German shoemaker with an untaught passion for art,\r\nthe other is a simple hearted old Yankee sailor-man whose possibilities\r\nare strictly limited to his ship, his cannon and his patch of petrified\r\nsea. They work these things up from twenty-five-cent tintypes; they get\r\nsix dollars apiece for them, and they can grind out a couple a day when\r\nthey strike what they call a boost\xe2\x80\x94that is, an inspiration.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeople actually pay money for these calumnies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey actually do\xe2\x80\x94and quite willingly, too. And these abortionists\r\ncould double their trade and work the women in, if Capt. Saltmarsh\r\ncould whirl a horse in, or a piano, or a guitar, in place of his\r\ncannon. The fact is, he fatigues the market with that cannon. Even the\r\nmale market, I mean. These fourteen in the procession are not all\r\nsatisfied. One is an old \xe2\x80\x98independent\xe2\x80\x99 fireman, and he wants an engine\r\nin place of the cannon; another is a mate of a tug, and wants a tug in\r\nplace of the ship \xe2\x80\x94and so on, and so on. But the captain can\xe2\x80\x99t make a\r\ntug that is deceptive, and a fire engine is many flights beyond his\r\npower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis _is_ a most extraordinary form of robbery, I never have heard of\r\nanything like it. It\xe2\x80\x99s interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and so are the artists. They are perfectly honest men, and\r\nsincere. And the old sailor-man is full of sound religion, and is as\r\ndevoted a student of the Bible and misquoter of it as you can find\r\nanywhere. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know a better man or kinder hearted old soul than\r\nSaltmarsh, although he does swear a little, sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe seems to be perfect. I want to know him, Barrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have the chance. I guess I hear them coming, now. We\xe2\x80\x99ll draw\r\nthem out on their art, if you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe artists arrived and shook hands with great heartiness. The German\r\nwas forty and a little fleshy, with a shiny bald head and a kindly face\r\nand deferential manner. Capt. Saltmarsh was sixty, tall, erect,\r\npowerfully built, with coal-black hair and whiskers, and he had a well\r\ntanned complexion, and a gait and countenance that were full of\r\ncommand, confidence and decision. His horny hands and wrists were\r\ncovered with tattoo-marks, and when his lips parted, his teeth showed\r\nup white and blemishless. His voice was the effortless deep bass of a\r\nchurch organ, and would disturb the tranquility of a gas flame fifty\r\nyards away.\r\n\r\n\r\np165.jpg (16K)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re wonderful pictures,\xe2\x80\x9d said Barrow. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve been examining them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very bleasant dot you like dem,\xe2\x80\x9d said Handel, the German,\r\ngreatly pleased. \xe2\x80\x9cUnd you, Herr Tracy, you haf peen bleased mit dem\r\ntoo, alretty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can honestly say I have never seen anything just like them before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSch\xc3\xb6n!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the German, delighted. \xe2\x80\x9cYou hear, Gaptain? Py crashus, of he could do a clavier, of he could do\r\na guitar, of he could do a vashtub, it is a fortune, heiliger Yohanniss\r\nit is yoost a fortune!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is an immense pity that the business is hindered and limited\r\nin this unfortunate way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe captain grew a trifle excited, himself, now:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve said it, Mr. Tracy!\xe2\x80\x94Hindered? well, I should say so. He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m troubled about him. He never would have\r\nmade a break like that if he hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been a little off his balance. But\r\nI know what being out of work and no prospect ahead can do for a man.\r\nFirst it knocks the pluck out of him and drags his pride in the dirt;\r\nworry does the rest, and his mind gets shaky. I must talk to these\r\npeople. No\xe2\x80\x94if there\xe2\x80\x99s any humanity in them\xe2\x80\x94and there is, at\r\nbottom\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99ll be easier on him if they think his troubles have\r\ndisturbed his reason. Poor devil! away off here, and not a\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII.\r\n\r\n\r\nThe moment Tracy was alone his spirits vanished away, and all the\r\nmisery of his situation was manifest to him. To be moneyless and an\r\nobject of the chairmaker\xe2\x80\x99s charity\xe2\x80\x94this was bad enough, but his folly\r\nin proclaiming himself an earl\xe2\x80\x99s son to that scoffing and unbelieving\r\ncrew, and, on top of that, the humiliating result\xe2\x80\x94the recollection of\r\nthese things was a sharper torture still. He made up his mind that he\r\nwould never play earl\xe2\x80\x99s son again before a doubtful audience.\r\n\r\nHis father\xe2\x80\x99s answer was a blow he could not understand. At times he\r\nthought his father imagined he could get work to do in America without\r\nany trouble, and was minded to let him try it and cure himself of his\r\nradicalism by hard, cold, disenchanting experience. That seemed the\r\nmost plausible theory, yet he could not content himself with it. A\r\ntheory that pleased him better was, that this cablegram would be\r\nfollowed by another, of a gentler sort, requiring him to come home.\r\nShould he write and strike his flag, and ask for a ticket home? Oh, no,\r\nthat he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t _ever_ do. At least, not yet. That cablegram would\r\ncome, it certainly would. So he went from one telegraph office to\r\nanother every day for nearly a week, and asked if there was a cablegram\r\nfor Howard Tracy. No, there wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any. So they answered him at first.\r\nLater, they said it before he had a chance to ask. Later still they\r\nmerely shook their heads impatiently as soon as he came in sight. After\r\nthat he was ashamed to go any more.\r\n\r\nHe was down in the lowest depths of despair, now; for the harder Barrow\r\ntried to find work for him the more hopeless the possibilities seemed\r\nto grow. At last he said to Barrow:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here. I want to make a confession. I have got down, now, to where\r\nI am not only willing to acknowledge to myself that I am a shabby\r\ncreature and full of false pride, but am willing to acknowledge it to\r\nyou. Well, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been allowing you to wear yourself out hunting for work\r\nfor me when there\xe2\x80\x99s been a chance open to me all the time. Forgive my\r\npride\xe2\x80\x94what was left of it. It is all gone, now, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve come to\r\nconfess that if those ghastly artists want another confederate, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ntheir man\xe2\x80\x94for at last I am dead to shame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo? Really, can you paint?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot as badly as _they_. No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t claim that, for I am not a genius;\r\nin fact, I am a very indifferent amateur, a slouchy dabster, a mere\r\nartistic sarcasm; but drunk or asleep I can beat _those_ buccaneers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShake! I want to shout! Oh, I tell you, I am immensely delighted and\r\nrelieved. Oh, just to work\xe2\x80\x94that is life! No matter what the work\r\nis\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s of no consequence. Just work itself is bliss when a man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbeen starving for it. I\xe2\x80\x99ve _been_ there! Come right along; we\xe2\x80\x99ll hunt\r\nthe old boys up. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you feel good? I tell you _I_ do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe freebooters were not at home. But their \xe2\x80\x9cworks\xe2\x80\x9d were, displayed in\r\nprofusion all about the little ratty studio. Cannon to the right of\r\nthem, cannon to the left of them, cannon in front\xe2\x80\x94it was Balaclava come\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s the uncontented hackman, Tracy. Buckle to\xe2\x80\x94deepen the sea-green\r\nto turf, turn the ship into a hearse. Let the boys have a taste of your\r\nquality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe artists arrived just as the last touch was put on. They stood\r\ntransfixed with admiration.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy souls but she\xe2\x80\x99s a stunner, that hearse! The hackman will just go\r\nall to pieces when he sees that won\xe2\x80\x99t he Andy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is sphlennid, sphlennid! Herr Tracy, why haf you not said you\r\nvas a so sublime aartist? Lob\xe2\x80\x99 Gott, of you had lif\xe2\x80\x99d in Paris you\r\nwould be a Pree de Rome, dot\xe2\x80\x99s votes de matter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe arrangements were soon made. Tracy was taken into full and equal\r\npartnership, and he went straight to work, with dash and energy, to\r\nreconstructing gems of art whose accessories had failed to satisfy.\r\nUnder his hand, on that and succeeding days, artillery disappeared and\r\nthe emblems of peace and commerce took its place\xe2\x80\x94cats, hacks, sausages,\r\ntugs, fire engines, pianos, guitars, rocks, gardens, flower-pots,\r\nlandscapes\xe2\x80\x94whatever was wanted, he flung it in; and the more out of\r\nplace and absurd the required object was, the more joy he got out of\r\nfabricating it. The pirates were delighted, the customers applauded,\r\nthe sex began to flock in, great was the prosperity of the firm. Tracy\r\nwas obliged to confess to himself that there was something about\r\nwork,\xe2\x80\x94even such grotesque and humble work as this\xe2\x80\x94which most pleasantly\r\nsatisfied a something in his nature which had never been satisfied\r\nbefore, and also gave him a strange new dignity in his own private view\r\nof himself.\r\n\r\nThe Unqualified Member from Cherokee Strip was in a state of deep\r\ndejection. For a good while, now, he had been leading a sort of life\r\nwhich was calculated to kill; for it had consisted in regularly\r\nalternating days of brilliant hope and black disappointment. The\r\nbrilliant hopes were created by the magician Sellers, and they always\r\npromised that _now_ he had got the trick, sure, and would effectively\r\ninfluence that materialized cowboy to call at the Towers before night.\r\nThe black disappointments consisted in the persistent and monotonous\r\nfailure of these prophecies.\r\n\r\nAt the date which this history has now reached, Sellers was appalled to\r\nfind that the usual remedy was inoperative, and that Hawkins\xe2\x80\x99s low\r\nspirits refused absolutely to lift. Something must be done, he\r\nreflected; it was heart-breaking, this woe, this smileless misery, this\r\ndull despair that looked out from his poor friend\xe2\x80\x99s face. Yes, he must\r\nbe cheered up. He mused a while, then he saw his way. He said in his\r\nmost conspicuously casual vein:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEr\xe2\x80\x94uh\xe2\x80\x94by the way, Hawkins, we are feeling disappointed about this\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x94the way the materializee is acting, I mean\xe2\x80\x94we are disappointed;\r\nyou concede that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConcede it? Why, yes, if you like the term.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well; so far, so good. Now for the _basis_ of the feeling. It is\r\nnot that your heart, your affections are concerned; that is to say, it\r\nis not that you _want_ the materializee _Itself_. You concede that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I concede that, too\xe2\x80\x94cordially.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, again; we are making progress. To sum up: The feeling, it\r\nis conceded, is not engendered by the mere conduct of the materializee;\r\nit is conceded that it does not arise from any pang which the\r\n_personality_ of the materializee could assuage. Now then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nearl, with the light of triumph in his eye, \xe2\x80\x9cthe inexorable logic of\r\nthe situation narrows us down to this: our feeling has its source in\r\nthe _money_-loss involved. Come\xe2\x80\x94isn\xe2\x80\x99t that so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness knows I concede that, with all my heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well. When you\xe2\x80\x99ve found out the source of a disease, you\xe2\x80\x99ve also\r\nfound out what remedy is required\xe2\x80\x94just as in this case. In this case\r\nmoney is required. And _only_ money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old, old seduction was in that airy, confident tone and those\r\nsignificant words\xe2\x80\x94usually called pregnant words in books. The old\r\nanswering signs of faith and hope showed up in Hawkins\xe2\x80\x99s countenance,\r\nand he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Only_ money? Do you mean that you know a way to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWashington, have you the impression that I have no resources but those\r\nI allow the public and my intimate friends to know about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it _likely_, do you think, that a man moved by nature and taught by\r\nexperience to keep his affairs to himself and a cautious and reluctant\r\ntongue in his head, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be thoughtful enough to keep a few\r\nresources in reserve for a rainy day, when he\xe2\x80\x99s got as many as I have\r\nto select from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you make me feel so much better already, Colonel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever been in my laboratory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it. You see you didn\xe2\x80\x99t even know that I had one. Come along.\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a little trick there that I want to show you. I\xe2\x80\x99ve kept it\r\nperfectly quiet, not fifty people know anything about it. But that\xe2\x80\x99s my\r\nway, always been my way. Wait till you\xe2\x80\x99re _ready_, that\xe2\x80\x99s the idea; and\r\n_when_ you\xe2\x80\x99re ready, _zzip!_\xe2\x80\x94let her go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Colonel, I\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen a man that I\xe2\x80\x99ve had such unbounded\r\nconfidence in as you. When you say a thing right out, I always feel as\r\nif that ends it; as if that is evidence, and proof, and everything\r\nelse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old earl was profoundly pleased and touched.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m glad _you_ believe in me, Washington; not everybody is so just.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always have believed in you; and I always shall as long as I live.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, my boy. You shan\xe2\x80\x99t repent it. And you _can\xe2\x80\x99t_.\xe2\x80\x9d Arrived in\r\nthe \xe2\x80\x9claboratory,\xe2\x80\x9d the earl continued, \xe2\x80\x9cNow, cast your eye around this\r\nroom\xe2\x80\x94what do you see? _Apparently_ a junk-shop; _apparently_ a hospital\r\nconnected with a patent office\xe2\x80\x94in _reality_, the mines of Golconda in\r\ndisguise! Look at that thing there. Now what would you take that thing\r\nto be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe I could ever imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t. It\xe2\x80\x99s my grand adaptation of the phonograph to\r\nthe marine service. You store up profanity in it for use at sea. You\r\nknow that sailors don\xe2\x80\x99t fly around worth a cent unless you swear at\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94so the mate that can do the best job of swearing is the most\r\nvaluable man. In great emergencies his talent saves the ship. But a\r\nship is a large thing, and he can\xe2\x80\x99t be everywhere at once; so there\r\nhave been times when one mate has lost a ship which could have been\r\nsaved if they had had a hundred. Prodigious storms, you know. Well, a\r\nship can\xe2\x80\x99t afford a hundred mates; but she can afford a hundred Cursing\r\nPhonographs, and distribute them all over the vessel\xe2\x80\x94and there, you\r\nsee, she\xe2\x80\x99s armed at every point. Imagine a big storm, and a hundred of\r\nmy machines all cursing away at once\xe2\x80\x94splendid spectacle, splendid!\xe2\x80\x94you\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear yourself think. Ship goes through that storm perfectly\r\nserene\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s just as safe as she\xe2\x80\x99d be on shore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a wonderful idea. How do you prepare the thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLoad it\xe2\x80\x94simply load it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy you just stand over it and swear into it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat loads it, does it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94because every word it collars, it _keeps_\xe2\x80\x94keeps it forever. Never\r\nwears out. Any time you turn the crank, out it\xe2\x80\x99ll come. In times of\r\ngreat peril, you can reverse it, and it\xe2\x80\x99ll swear backwards. _That_\r\nmakes a sailor hump himself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, I see. Who loads them?\xe2\x80\x94the mate?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if he chooses. Or I\xe2\x80\x99ll furnish them already loaded. I can hire an\r\nexpert for $75 a month who will load a hundred and fifty phonographs in\r\n150 hours, and do it _easy_. And an expert can furnish a stronger\r\narticle, of course, than the mere average uncultivated mate could. Then\r\nyou see, all the ships of the world will buy them ready loaded\xe2\x80\x94for I\r\nshall have them loaded in any language a customer wants. Hawkins, it\r\nwill work the grandest moral reform of the 19th century. Five years\r\nfrom now, _all_ the swearing will be done by machinery\xe2\x80\x94you won\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\nhear a profane word come from human lips on a ship. Millions of dollars\r\nhave been spent by the churches, in the effort to abolish profanity in\r\nthe commercial marine. Think of it\xe2\x80\x94my name will live forever in the\r\naffections of good men as the man, who, solitary and alone,\r\naccomplished this noble and elevating reform.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, it _is_ grand and beneficent and beautiful. How _did_ you ever come\r\nto think of it? You have a wonderful mind. How did you say you loaded\r\nthe machine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, it\xe2\x80\x99s no trouble\xe2\x80\x94perfectly simple. If you want to load it up loud\r\nand strong, you stand right over it and shout. But if you leave it open\r\nand all set, it\xe2\x80\x99ll _eavesdrop_, so to speak\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, it will\r\nload itself up with any sounds that are made within six feet of it. Now\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll show you how it works. I had an expert come and load this one up\r\nyesterday. Hello, it\xe2\x80\x99s been left open\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s too bad\xe2\x80\x94still I reckon it\r\nhasn\xe2\x80\x99t had much chance to collect irrelevant stuff. All you do is to\r\npress this button in the floor\xe2\x80\x94so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe phonograph began to sing in a plaintive voice:\r\n\r\nThere is a boarding-house, far far away,\r\nWhere they have ham and eggs, 3 times a day.\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHang it, _that_ ain\xe2\x80\x99t it. Somebody\xe2\x80\x99s been singing around here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe plaintive song began again, mingled with a low, gradually rising\r\nwail of cats slowly warming up toward a fight;\r\n\r\nO, _how_ the boarders yell,\r\nWhen they hear that dinner bell\r\nThey give that landlord\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n(momentary outburst of terrific catfight which drowns out one word.)\r\n\r\nThree times a day.\r\n\r\n\r\n(Renewal of furious catfight for a moment. The plaintive voice on a\r\nhigh fierce key, \xe2\x80\x9c_Scat_, you devils\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and a racket as of flying\r\nmissiles.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, never mind\xe2\x80\x94let it go. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got some sailor-profanity down in\r\nthere somewhere, if I could get to it. But it isn\xe2\x80\x99t any matter; you see\r\nhow the machine works.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHawkins responded with enthusiasm:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, it works admirably! I know there\xe2\x80\x99s a hundred fortunes in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd mind, the Hawkins family get their share, Washington.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, thanks, thanks; you are just as generous as ever. Ah, it\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\ngrandest invention of the age!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well; we live in wonderful times. The elements are crowded _full_\r\nof beneficent forces\xe2\x80\x94always _have_ been\xe2\x80\x94and ours is the first\r\ngeneration to turn them to account and make them work for us. Why\r\nHawkins, _everything_ is useful\xe2\x80\x94_nothing_ ought ever to be wasted. Now\r\nlook at sewer gas, for instance. Sewer gas has always been wasted,\r\nheretofore; nobody tried to save up sewer-gas\xe2\x80\x94you can\xe2\x80\x99t name me a man.\r\nAin\xe2\x80\x99t that so? _you_ know perfectly well it\xe2\x80\x99s so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes it is so\xe2\x80\x94but I never\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite see why a body\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould _want_ to save it up? Well, I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you. Do you see this\r\nlittle invention here?\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s a decomposer\xe2\x80\x94I call it a decomposer. I give\r\nyou my word of honor that if you show me a house that produces a given\r\nquantity of sewer-gas in a day, I\xe2\x80\x99ll engage to set up my decomposer\r\nthere and make that house produce a hundred times that quantity of\r\nsewer-gas in less than half an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me, but why should you want to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Want_ to? Listen, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll see. My boy, for illuminating purposes\r\nand economy combined, there\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in the world that begins with\r\nsewer-gas. And really, it don\xe2\x80\x99t cost a cent. You put in a good inferior\r\narticle of plumbing,\xe2\x80\x94such as you find everywhere\xe2\x80\x94and add my decomposer,\r\nand there you are. Just use the ordinary gas pipes\xe2\x80\x94and there your\r\nexpense ends. Think of it. Why, Major, in five years from now you won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsee a house lighted with anything but sewer-gas. Every physician I talk\r\nto, recommends it; and every plumber.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut isn\xe2\x80\x99t it dangerous?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, yes, more or less, but everything is\xe2\x80\x94coal gas, candles, electricity\r\n\xe2\x80\x94there isn\xe2\x80\x99t anything that ain\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt lights up well, does it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, magnificently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you given it a good trial?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no, not a first rate one. Polly\xe2\x80\x99s prejudiced, and she won\xe2\x80\x99t let\r\nme put it in here; but I\xe2\x80\x99m playing my cards to get it adopted in the\r\nPresident\xe2\x80\x99s house, and _then_ it\xe2\x80\x99ll go\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you doubt it. I shall not\r\nneed this one for the present, Washington; you may take it down to some\r\nboarding-house and give it a trial if you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nWashington shuddered slightly at the suggestion, then his face took on\r\na dreamy look and he dropped into a trance of thought. After a little,\r\nSellers asked him what he was grinding in his mental mill.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, this. Have you got some secret project in your head which\r\nrequires a Bank of England back of it to make it succeed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np184.jpg (28K)\r\n\r\nThe Colonel showed lively astonishment, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Hawkins, are you a mind-reader?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? I never thought of such a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then how did you happen to drop onto that idea in this curious\r\nfashion? It\xe2\x80\x99s just mind-reading, that\xe2\x80\x99s what it is, though you may not\r\nknow it. Because I _have_ got a private project that requires a Bank of\r\nEngland at its back. How could you divine that? What was the process?\r\nThis is interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any process. A thought like this happened to slip through\r\nmy head by accident: How much would make you or me comfortable? A\r\nhundred thousand. Yet you are expecting two or three of\xe2\x80\x94these\r\ninventions of yours to turn out some billions of money\xe2\x80\x94and you are\r\n_wanting_ them to do that. If you wanted ten millions, I could\r\nunderstand that\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s inside the human limits. But billions! That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclear outside the limits. There must be a definite project back of that\r\nsomewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe earl\xe2\x80\x99s interest and surprise augmented with every word, and when\r\nHawkins finished, he said with strong admiration:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s wonderfully reasoned out, Washington, it certainly is. It shows\r\nwhat I think is quite extraordinary penetration. For you\xe2\x80\x99ve hit it;\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve driven the centre, you\xe2\x80\x99ve plugged the bulls-eye of my dream. Now\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99ll tell you the whole thing, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll understand it. I don\xe2\x80\x99t need\r\nto ask you to keep it to yourself, because you\xe2\x80\x99ll see that the project\r\nwill prosper all the better for being kept in the background till the\r\nright time. Have you noticed how many pamphlets and books I\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\nlying around relating to Russia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I think most anybody would notice that\xe2\x80\x94anybody who wasn\xe2\x80\x99t dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve been posting myself a good while. That\xe2\x80\x99s a great and,\r\nsplendid nation, and deserves to be set free.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused, then added in\r\na quite matter-of-fact way, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I get this money I\xe2\x80\x99m going to set it\r\nfree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat guns!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what makes you jump like that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me, when you are going to drop a remark under a man\xe2\x80\x99s chair that\r\nis likely to blow him out through the roof, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you put some\r\nexpression, some force, some noise into it that will prepare him? You\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t flip out such a gigantic thing as this in that colorless kind\r\nof a way. You do jolt a person up, so. Go on, now, I am all right\r\nagain. Tell me all about it. I\xe2\x80\x99m all interest\xe2\x80\x94yes, and sympathy, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve looked the ground over, and concluded that the methods of\r\nthe Russian patriots, while good enough considering the way the boys\r\nare hampered, are not the best; at least not the quickest. They are\r\ntrying to revolutionize Russia from _within;_ that\xe2\x80\x99s pretty slow, you\r\nknow, and liable to interruption all the time, and is full of perils\r\nfor the workers. Do you know how Peter the Great started his army? He\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t start it on the family premises under the noses of the\r\nStrelitzes; no, he started it away off yonder, privately,\xe2\x80\x94only just one\r\nregiment, you know, and he built to _that_. The first thing the\r\nStrelitzes knew, the regiment was an _army_, their position was turned,\r\nand they had to take a walk. Just that little idea _made_ the biggest\r\nand worst of all the despotisms the world has seen. The same idea can\r\n_un_make it. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to prove it. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to get out to one side\r\nand work my scheme the way Peter did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is mighty interesting, Rossmore. What is it you are going to do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going to buy Siberia and start a republic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x94bang you go again, without giving any notice! Going to _buy_\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, as soon as I get the money. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care what the price is, I\r\nshall take it. I can afford it, and I will. Now then, consider this\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99ve never thought of it, I\xe2\x80\x99ll warrant. Where is the place where\r\nthere is twenty-five times more manhood, pluck, true heroism,\r\nunselfishness, devotion to high and noble ideals, adoration of liberty,\r\nwide education, and _brains_, per thousand of population, than any\r\nother domain in the whole world can show?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSiberia!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true; it certainly is true, but I never thought of it before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNobody ever thinks of it. But it\xe2\x80\x99s so, just the same. In those mines\r\nand prisons are gathered together the very finest and noblest and\r\ncapablest multitude of human beings that God is able to create. Now if\r\nyou had that kind of a population to sell, would you offer it to a\r\ndespotism? No, the despotism has no use for it; you would lose money. A\r\ndespotism has no use for anything but human cattle. But suppose you\r\nwant to start a republic?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I see. It\xe2\x80\x99s just the material for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I should say so! There\xe2\x80\x99s Siberia with just the very finest and\r\nchoicest material on the globe for a republic, and more coming\xe2\x80\x94more\r\ncoming all the time, don\xe2\x80\x99t you see! It is being daily, weekly, monthly\r\nrecruited by the most perfectly devised system that has ever been\r\ninvented, perhaps. By this system the whole of the hundred millions of\r\nRussia are being constantly and patiently sifted, sifted, sifted, by\r\nmyriads of trained experts, spies appointed by the Emperor personally;\r\nand whenever they catch a man, woman or child that has got any brains\r\nor education or character, they ship that person straight to Siberia.\r\nIt is admirable, it is wonderful. It is so searching and so effective\r\nthat it keeps the general level of Russian intellect and education down\r\nto that of the Czar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, that sounds like exaggeration.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s what they say anyway. But I think, myself, it\xe2\x80\x99s a lie. And\r\nit doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem right to slander a whole nation that way, anyhow. Now,\r\nthen, you see what the material is, there in Siberia, for a republic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nHe paused, and his breast began to heave and his eye to burn, under the\r\nimpulse of strong emotion. Then his words began to stream forth, with\r\nconstantly increasing energy and fire, and he rose to his feet as if to\r\ngive himself larger freedom. \xe2\x80\x9cThe minute I organize that republic, the\r\nlight of liberty, intelligence, justice, humanity, bursting from it,\r\nflooding from it, flaming from it, will concentrate the gaze of the\r\nwhole astonished world as upon the miracle of a new sun; Russia\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncountless multitudes of slaves will rise up and march, march!\xe2\x80\x94eastward,\r\nwith that great light transfiguring their faces as they come, and far\r\nback of them you will see-what will you see?\xe2\x80\x94a vacant throne in an\r\nempty land! It can be done, and by God I will do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np187.jpg (52K)\r\n\r\nHe stood a moment bereft of earthly consciousness by his exaltation;\r\nthen consciousness returned, bringing him a slight shock, and he said\r\nwith grave earnestness:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must ask you to pardon me, Major Hawkins. I have never used that\r\nexpression before, and I beg you will forgive it this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHawkins was quite willing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Washington, it is an error which I am by nature not liable\r\nto. Only excitable people, impulsive people, are exposed to it. See?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nQuite true. Tracy, out for an afternoon stroll, had come unexpectantly\r\nupon his family arms displayed upon this shabby house-front. The\r\nhatchments made him smile; which was nothing, they had made the\r\nneighborhood cats do that.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook, Hawkins, look! The carrying on of the\r\nmaterialization\xe2\x80\x94the bringing it down to date. I will begin on that at\r\nonce. I think\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Rossmore. A hundred to one it has\r\nescaped!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCalm yourself, as to that; don\xe2\x80\x99t give yourself any uneasiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t it escape?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet it, if it wants to. What of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, _I_ should consider it a pretty serious calamity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, my dear boy, once in my power, always in my power. I hope to persuade it to remain pretty\r\nquiet, though, because a materialization which is in a state of\r\narrested development must of necessity be pretty soft and flabby and\r\nsubstanceless, and\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94by the way, I wonder where It comes from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow? Finally she said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter with you, Mulberry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe did not answer immediately. Take me\r\naway\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it.\r\n\r\nThey tottered back to the laboratory.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX.\r\n\r\n\r\nTracy made slow progress with his work, for his mind wandered a good\r\ndeal. Many things were puzzling him. Finally a light burst upon him all\r\nof a sudden\xe2\x80\x94seemed to, at any rate\xe2\x80\x94and he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\nthe clew at last\xe2\x80\x94this man\xe2\x80\x99s mind is off its balance; I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how\r\nmuch, but it\xe2\x80\x99s off a point or two, sure; off enough to explain this\r\nmess of perplexities, anyway. These dreadful chromos which he takes for\r\nold masters; these villainous portraits\xe2\x80\x94which to his frantic mind\r\nrepresent Rossmores; the hatchments; the pompous name of this\r\nramshackle old crib\xe2\x80\x94Rossmore Towers; and that odd assertion of his,\r\nthat I was expected. How could I be expected? that is, Lord Berkeley.\r\nHe knows by the papers that that person was burned up in the New\r\nGadsby. Why, hang it, he really doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know _who_ he was expecting;\r\nfor his talk showed that he was not expecting an Englishman, or yet an\r\nartist, yet I answer his requirements notwithstanding. He seems\r\nsufficiently satisfied with me. Yes, he is a little off; in fact I am\r\nafraid he is a good deal off, poor old gentleman. But he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninteresting\xe2\x80\x94all people in about his condition are, I suppose. I hope\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99ll like my work; I would like to come every day and study him. And\r\nwhen I write my father\xe2\x80\x94ah, that hurts! I mustn\xe2\x80\x99t get on that subject;\r\nit isn\xe2\x80\x99t good for my spirits. Somebody coming\xe2\x80\x94I must get to work. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe old gentleman again. He looks bothered. Maybe my clothes are\r\nsuspicious; and they are\xe2\x80\x94for an artist. If my conscience would allow me\r\nto make a change, but that is out of the question. I wonder what he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmaking those passes in the air for, with his hands. I seem to be the\r\nobject of them. Can he be trying to mesmerize me? I don\xe2\x80\x99t quite like\r\nit. There\xe2\x80\x99s something uncanny about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe colonel muttered to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cIt has an effect on him, I can see it\r\nmyself. I\xe2\x80\x99ll just put a sly question\r\nor two at him, now, and see if I can find out what his condition is,\r\nand where he\xe2\x80\x99s from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe approached and said affably:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t let me disturb you, Mr. Tracy; I only want to take a little\r\nglimpse of your work. Ah, that\xe2\x80\x99s fine\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s very fine indeed. You are\r\ndoing it elegantly. My daughter will be charmed with this. I\xe2\x80\x99m glad I got him out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe sat thinking, and thinking, and watching the brush go. At length he\r\nsaid to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it certainly seems to account for the failure of\r\nmy endeavors in poor Berkeley\xe2\x80\x99s case. _He_ went in the other direction.\r\nWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s all right. He\xe2\x80\x99s better off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSally Sellers entered from the street, now, looking her divinest, and\r\nthe artist was introduced to her. It was a violent case of mutual love\r\nat first sight, though neither party was entirely aware of the fact,\r\nperhaps. The Englishman made this irrelevant remark to himself,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps he is not insane, after all.\xe2\x80\x9d Sally sat down, and showed an\r\ninterest in Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s work which greatly pleased him, and a benevolent\r\nforgiveness of it which convinced him that the girl\xe2\x80\x99s nature was cast\r\nin a large mould. Sellers was anxious to report his discoveries to\r\nHawkins; so he took his leave, saying that if the two \xe2\x80\x9cyoung devotees\r\nof the colored Muse\xe2\x80\x9d thought they could manage without him, he would go\r\nand look after his affairs. The artist said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI think he is\r\na little eccentric, perhaps, but that is all.\xe2\x80\x9d He reproached himself\r\nfor having injuriously judged a man without giving him any fair chance\r\nto show what he really was.\r\n\r\n\r\np204.jpg (29K)\r\n\r\nOf course the stranger was very soon at his ease and chatting along\r\ncomfortably. The average American girl possesses the valuable qualities\r\nof naturalness, honesty, and inoffensive straightforwardness; she is\r\nnearly barren of troublesome conventions and artificialities,\r\nconsequently her presence and her ways are unembarrassing, and one is\r\nacquainted with her and on the pleasantest terms with her before he\r\nknows how it came about. This new acquaintanceship\xe2\x80\x94friendship,\r\nindeed\xe2\x80\x94progressed swiftly; and the unusual swiftness of it, and the\r\nthoroughness of it are sufficiently evidenced and established by one\r\nnoteworthy fact\xe2\x80\x94that within the first half hour both parties had ceased\r\nto be conscious of Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s clothes. Later this consciousness was\r\nre-awakened; it was then apparent to Gwendolen that she was almost\r\nreconciled to them, and it was apparent to Tracy that he wasn\xe2\x80\x99t. The\r\nre-awakening was brought about by Gwendolen\xe2\x80\x99s inviting the artist to\r\nstay to dinner. He had to decline, because he wanted to live, now\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nis, now that there was something to live for\xe2\x80\x94and he could not survive\r\nin those clothes at a gentleman\xe2\x80\x99s table. He thought he knew that. But\r\nhe went away happy, for he saw that Gwendolen was disappointed.\r\n\r\nAnd whither did he go? He went straight to a slopshop and bought as\r\nneat and reasonably well-fitting a suit of clothes as an Englishman\r\ncould be persuaded to wear. He said\xe2\x80\x94_to_ himself, but _at_ his\r\nconscience\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cI know it\xe2\x80\x99s wrong; but it would be wrong _not_ to do it;\r\nand two wrongs do not make a right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis satisfied him, and made his heart light. Perhaps it will also\r\nsatisfy the reader\xe2\x80\x94if he can make out what it means.\r\n\r\nThe old people were troubled about Gwendolen at dinner, because she was\r\nso distraught and silent. If they had noticed, they would have found\r\nthat she was sufficiently alert and interested whenever the talk\r\nstumbled upon the artist and his work; but they didn\xe2\x80\x99t notice, and so\r\nthe chat would swap around to some other subject, and then somebody\r\nwould presently be privately worrying about Gwendolen again, and\r\nwondering if she were not well, or if something had gone wrong in the\r\nmillinery line. Her mother offered her various reputable patent\r\nmedicines, and tonics with iron and other hardware in them, and her\r\nfather even proposed to send out for wine, relentless prohibitionist\r\nand head of the order in the District of Columbia as he was, but these\r\nkindnesses were all declined\xe2\x80\x94thankfully, but with decision. At bedtime,\r\nwhen the family were breaking up for the night, she privately looted\r\none of the brushes, saying to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the one he has used, the\r\nmost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next morning Tracy went forth wearing his new suit, and equipped\r\nwith a pink in his button-hole\xe2\x80\x94a daily attention from Puss. His whole\r\nsoul was full of Gwendolen Sellers, and this condition was an\r\ninspiration, art-wise. All the morning his brush pawed nimbly away at\r\nthe canvases, almost without his awarity\xe2\x80\x94awarity, in this sense being\r\nthe sense of being aware, though disputed by some authorities\xe2\x80\x94turning\r\nout marvel upon marvel, in the way of decorative accessories to the\r\nportraits, with a felicity and celerity which amazed the veterans of\r\nthe firm and fetched out of them continuous explosions of applause.\r\n\r\nMeantime Gwendolen was losing her morning, and many dollars. She\r\nsupposed Tracy was coming in the forenoon\xe2\x80\x94a conclusion which she had\r\njumped to without outside help. So she tripped down stairs every little\r\nwhile from her work-parlor to arrange the brushes and things over\r\nagain, and see if he had arrived. And when she was in her work-parlor\r\nit was not profitable, but just the other way\xe2\x80\x94as she found out to her\r\nsorrow.\r\n\r\nShe had put in her idle moments during the last little while back, in\r\ndesigning a particularly rare and capable gown for herself, and this\r\nmorning she set about making it up; but she was absent minded, and made\r\nan irremediable botch of it. When she saw what she had done, she knew\r\nthe reason of it and the meaning of it; and she put her work away from\r\nher and said she would accept the sign. And from that time forth she\r\ncame no more away from the Audience Chamber, but remained there and\r\nwaited. After luncheon she waited again. A whole hour. Then a great joy\r\nwelled up in her heart, for she saw him coming. So she flew back up\r\nstairs thankful, and could hardly wait for him to miss the principal\r\nbrush, which she had mislaid down there, but knew where she had mislaid\r\nit. However, all in good time the others were called in and couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nfind the brush, and then she was sent for, and she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find it\r\nherself for some little time; but then she found it when the others had\r\ngone away to hunt in the kitchen and down cellar and in the woodshed,\r\nand all those other places where people look for things whose ways they\r\nare not familiar with. So she gave him the brush, and remarked that she\r\nought to have seen that everything was ready for him, but it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nseemed necessary, because it was so early that she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t expecting\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nshe stopped there, surprised at herself for what she was saying; and he\r\nfelt caught and ashamed, and said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI knew my impatience\r\nwould drag me here before I was expected, and betray me, and that is\r\njust what it has done; she sees straight through me\xe2\x80\x94and is laughing at\r\nme, inside, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGwendolen was very much pleased, on one account, and a little the other\r\nway in another; pleased with the new clothes and the improvement which\r\nthey had achieved; less pleased by the pink in the buttonhole.\r\nYesterday\xe2\x80\x99s pink had hardly interested her; this one was just like it,\r\nbut somehow it had got her immediate attention, and kept it. She wished\r\nshe could think of some way of getting at its history in a properly\r\ncolorless and indifferent way. Presently she made a venture. She said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhatever a man\xe2\x80\x99s age may be, he can reduce it several years by putting\r\na bright-colored flower in his button-hole. I have often noticed that.\r\nIs that your sex\xe2\x80\x99s reason for wearing a boutonniere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy not, but certainly that reason would be a sufficient one. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nnever heard of the idea before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to prefer pinks. Is it on account of the color, or the form?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh no,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, simply, \xe2\x80\x9cthey are given to me. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I have\r\nany preference.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are given to him,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to herself, and she felt a coldness\r\ntoward that pink. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder who it is, and what she is like.\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nflower began to take up a good deal of room; it obtruded itself\r\neverywhere, it intercepted all views, and marred them; it was becoming\r\nexceedingly annoying and conspicuous for a little thing. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if\r\nhe cares for her.\xe2\x80\x9d That thought gave her a quite definite pain.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI.\r\n\r\n\r\nShe had made everything comfortable for the artist; there was no\r\nfurther pretext for staying. So she said she would go, now, and asked\r\nhim to summon the servants in case he should need anything. She went\r\naway unhappy; and she left unhappiness behind her; for she carried away\r\nall the sunshine. The time dragged heavily for both, now. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\npaint for thinking of her; she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t design or millinerize with any\r\nheart, for thinking of him. Never before had painting seemed so empty\r\nto him, never before had millinerizing seemed so void of interest to\r\nher. She had gone without repeating that dinner-invitation\xe2\x80\x94an almost\r\nunendurable disappointment to him. On her part-well, she was suffering,\r\ntoo; for she had found she _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ invite him. It was not hard\r\nyesterday, but it was impossible to-day. A thousand innocent privileges\r\nseemed to have been filched from her unawares in the past twenty-four\r\nhours. To-day she felt strangely hampered, restrained of her liberty.\r\nTo-day she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t propose to herself to do anything or say anything\r\nconcerning this young man without being instantly paralyzed into\r\nnon-action by the fear that he might \xe2\x80\x9csuspect.\xe2\x80\x9d Invite him to dinner\r\n_to-day?_ It made her shiver to think of it.\r\n\r\nAnd so her afternoon was one long fret. Broken at intervals. Three\r\ntimes she had to go down stairs on errands\xe2\x80\x94that is, she thought she had\r\nto go down stairs on errands. Thus, going and coming, she had six\r\nglimpses of him, in the aggregate, without seeming to look in his\r\ndirection; and she tried to endure these electric ecstasies without\r\nshowing any sign, but they fluttered her up a good deal, and she felt\r\nthat the naturalness she was putting on was overdone and quite too\r\nfrantically sober and hysterically calm to deceive.\r\n\r\nThe painter had his share of the rapture; he had his six glimpses, and\r\nthey smote him with waves of pleasure that assaulted him, beat upon\r\nhim, washed over him deliciously, and drowned out all consciousness of\r\nwhat he was doing with his brush. So there were six places in his\r\ncanvas which had to be done over again.\r\n\r\n\r\np210.jpg (23K)\r\n\r\nAt last Gwendolen got some peace of mind by sending word to the\r\nThompsons, in the neighborhood, that she was coming there to dinner.\r\nShe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be reminded, at _that_ table, that there was an absentee\r\nwho ought to be a presentee\xe2\x80\x94a word which she meant to look out in the\r\ndictionary at a calmer time.\r\n\r\nAbout this time the old earl dropped in for a chat with the artist, and\r\ninvited him to stay to dinner. Tracy cramped down his joy and gratitude\r\nby a sudden and powerful exercise of all his forces; and he felt that\r\nnow that he was going to be close to Gwendolen, and hear her voice and\r\nwatch her face during several precious hours, earth had nothing\r\nvaluable to add to his life for the present.\r\n\r\nThe earl said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cThis spectre can eat apples, apparently. We\r\nshall find out, now, if that is a specialty. I think, myself, it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nspecialty. Apples, without doubt, constitute the spectral limit. It was\r\nthe case with our first parents. No, I am wrong\xe2\x80\x94at least only partly\r\nright. The line _was_ drawn at apples, just as in the present case, but\r\nit was from the other direction.\xe2\x80\x9d The new clothes gave him a thrill of\r\npleasure and pride. Perhaps the\r\nmost extraordinary mind since Aristotle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDinner was kept waiting for a while for Miss Thompson, but as Gwendolen\r\nhad not delivered the invitation to her the waiting did no good, and\r\nthe household presently went to the meal without her. The idolaters were the width of the room apart; and\r\napparently unconscious of each other\xe2\x80\x99s presence. The distance got\r\nshortened a little, now. Very soon the mother withdrew. The distance\r\nnarrowed again. Tracy stood before a chromo of some Ohio politician\r\nwhich had been retouched and chain-mailed for a crusading Rossmore, and\r\nGwendolen was sitting on the sofa not far from his elbow artificially\r\nabsorbed in examining a photograph album that hadn\xe2\x80\x99t any photographs in\r\nit.\r\n\r\nThe \xe2\x80\x9cSenator\xe2\x80\x9d still lingered. He was sorry for the young people; it had\r\nbeen a dull evening for them. In the goodness of his heart he tried to\r\nmake it pleasant for them now; tried to remove the ill impression\r\nnecessarily left by the general defeat; tried to be chatty, even tried\r\nto be gay. But the responses were sickly, there was no starting any\r\nenthusiasm; he would give it up and quit\xe2\x80\x94it was a day specially picked\r\nout and consecrated to failures.\r\n\r\nBut when Gwendolen rose up promptly and smiled a glad smile and said\r\nwith thankfulness and blessing,\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9c_Must_ you go?\xe2\x80\x9d it seemed cruel to\r\ndesert, and he sat down again.\r\n\r\nHe was about to begin a remark when\xe2\x80\x94when he didn\xe2\x80\x99t. We have all been\r\nthere. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t know how he knew his concluding to stay longer had\r\nbeen a mistake, he merely knew it; and knew it for dead certain, too.\r\nAnd so he bade goodnight, and went mooning out, wondering what he could\r\nhave done that changed the atmosphere that way. As the door closed\r\nbehind him those two were standing side by side, looking at that\r\ndoor\xe2\x80\x94looking at it in a waiting, second-counting, but deeply grateful\r\nkind of way. And the instant it closed they flung their arms about each\r\nother\xe2\x80\x99s necks, and there, heart to heart and lip to lip\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my God, she\xe2\x80\x99s kissing it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNobody heard this remark, because Hawkins, who bred it, only thought\r\nit, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t utter it. He had turned, the moment he had closed the\r\ndoor, and had pushed it open a little, intending to re-enter and ask\r\nwhat ill-advised thing he had done or said, and apologize for it. But\r\nhe didn\xe2\x80\x99t re-enter; he staggered off stunned, terrified, distressed.\r\n\r\n\r\np222.jpg (33K)\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII.\r\n\r\n\r\nFive minutes later he was sitting in his room, with his head bowed\r\nwithin the circle of his arms, on the table\xe2\x80\x94final attitude of grief and\r\ndespair. His tears were flowing fast, and now and then a sob broke upon\r\nthe stillness. Presently he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew her when she was a little child and used to climb about my\r\nknees; I love her as I love my own, and now\xe2\x80\x94oh, poor thing, poor thing,\r\nI cannot bear it!\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s gone and lost her heart to this mangy\r\nmaterializee! Why _didn\xe2\x80\x99t_ we see that that might happen? But how could\r\nwe? Nobody could; nobody could ever have dreamed of such a thing. You\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t expect a person would fall in love with a wax-work. And this\r\none doesn\xe2\x80\x99t even amount to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went on grieving to himself, and now and then giving voice to his\r\nlamentations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s done, oh, it\xe2\x80\x99s done, and there\xe2\x80\x99s no help for it, no undoing the\r\nmiserable business. If I had the nerve, I would kill it. But that\r\nwouldn\xe2\x80\x99t do any good. _She_ loves it; she thinks it\xe2\x80\x99s genuine and\r\nauthentic. It costs five thousand dollars cash and shuts\r\ndown on the incorporated company to stop the works at this point, but\r\nSally Sellers\xe2\x80\x99s happiness is worth more than that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe heard Sellers coming, and got himself to rights. Under that placid exterior is concealed\r\nthe most dare-devil spirit that was ever put into a man\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s just a\r\nClive over again. A tiff\r\nwas the result of the dispute between the two. Then they made it up,\r\nand were more loving than ever. Yes, he could have his girl and have his earldom, too: that\r\nquestion was a fortunate stroke.\r\n\r\nSally went to bed happy, too; and remained happy, deliriously happy,\r\nfor nearly two hours; but at last, just as she was sinking into a\r\ncontented and luxurious unconsciousness, the shady devil who lives and\r\nlurks and hides and watches inside of human beings and is always\r\nwaiting for a chance to do the proprietor a malicious damage, whispered\r\nto her soul and said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat question had a harmless look, but what was\r\n_back_ of it?\xe2\x80\x94what was the secret motive of it?\xe2\x80\x94what suggested it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe shady devil had knifed her, and could retire, now, and take a rest;\r\nthe wound would attend to business for him. And it did.\r\n\r\n\r\np234.jpg (24K)\r\n\r\n_Why_ should Howard Tracy ask that question? If he was not trying to\r\nmarry her for the sake of her rank, what should suggest that question\r\nto him? Didn\xe2\x80\x99t he plainly look gratified when she said her objections\r\nto aristocracy had their limitations? Ah, he is after that earldom,\r\nthat gilded sham\xe2\x80\x94it isn\xe2\x80\x99t poor me he wants.\r\n\r\nSo she argued, in anguish and tears. Then she argued the opposite\r\ntheory, but made a weak, poor business of it, and lost the case. She\r\nkept the arguing up, one side and then the other, the rest of the\r\nnight, and at last fell asleep at dawn; fell in the fire at dawn, one\r\nmay say; for that kind of sleep resembles fire, and one comes out of it\r\nwith his brain baked and his physical forces fried out of him.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII.\r\n\r\n\r\nTracy wrote his father before he sought his bed. He wrote a letter\r\nwhich he believed would get better treatment than his cablegram\r\nreceived, for it contained what ought to be welcome news; namely, that\r\nhe had tried equality and working for a living; had made a fight which\r\nhe could find no reason to be ashamed of, and in the matter of earning\r\na living had proved that he was able to do it; but that on the whole he\r\nhad arrived at the conclusion that he could not reform the world\r\nsingle-handed, and was willing to retire from the conflict with the\r\nfair degree of honor which he had gained, and was also willing to\r\nreturn home and resume his position and be content with it and thankful\r\nfor it for the future, leaving further experiment of a missionary sort\r\nto other young people needing the chastening and quelling persuasions\r\nof experience, the only logic sure to convince a diseased imagination\r\nand restore it to rugged health. Then he approached the subject of\r\nmarriage with the daughter of the American Claimant with a good deal of\r\ncaution and much painstaking art. He said praiseful and appreciative\r\nthings about the girl, but didn\xe2\x80\x99t dwell upon that detail or make it\r\nprominent. The thing which he made prominent was the opportunity now so\r\nhappily afforded, to reconcile York and Lancaster, graft the warring\r\nroses upon one stem, and end forever a crying injustice which had\r\nalready lasted far too long. One could infer that he had thought this\r\nthing all out and chosen this way of making all things fair and right\r\nbecause it was sufficiently fair and considerably wiser than the\r\nrenunciation-scheme which he had brought with him from England. One\r\ncould infer that, but he didn\xe2\x80\x99t say it. In fact the more he read his\r\nletter over, the more he got to inferring it himself.\r\n\r\nWhen the old earl received that letter, the first part of it filled him\r\nwith a grim and snarly satisfaction; but the rest of it brought a snort\r\nor two out of him that could be translated differently. He wasted no\r\nink in this emergency, either in cablegrams or letters; he promptly\r\ntook ship for America to look into the matter himself. He had staunchly\r\nheld his grip all this long time, and given no sign of the hunger at\r\nhis heart to see his son; hoping for the cure of his insane dream, and\r\nresolute that the process should go through all the necessary stages\r\nwithout assuaging telegrams or other nonsense from home, and here was\r\nvictory at last. Victory, but stupidly marred by this idiotic marriage\r\nproject. Yes, he would step over and take a hand in this matter\r\nhimself.\r\n\r\nDuring the first ten days following the mailing of the letter Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nspirits had no idle time; they were always climbing up into the clouds\r\nor sliding down into the earth as deep as the law of gravitation\r\nreached. He was intensely happy or intensely miserable by turns,\r\naccording to Miss Sally\xe2\x80\x99s moods. He never could tell when the mood was\r\ngoing to change, and when it changed he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell what it was that\r\nhad changed it. Sometimes she was so in love with him that her love was\r\ntropical, torrid, and she could find no language fervent enough for its\r\nexpression; then suddenly, and without warning or any apparent reason,\r\nthe weather would change, and the victim would find himself adrift\r\namong the icebergs and feeling as lonesome and friendless as the north\r\npole. It sometimes seemed to him that a man might better be dead than\r\nexposed to these devastating varieties of climate.\r\n\r\nThe case was simple. Sally _wanted_ to believe that Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s preference\r\nwas disinterested; so she was always applying little tests of one sort\r\nor another, hoping and expecting that they would bring out evidence\r\nwhich would confirm or fortify her belief. Poor Tracy did not know that\r\nthese experiments were being made upon him, consequently he walked\r\npromptly into all the traps the girl set for him. These traps consisted\r\nin apparently casual references to social distinction, aristocratic\r\ntitle and privilege, and such things. Often Tracy responded to these\r\nreferences heedlessly and not much caring what he said provided it kept\r\nthe talk going and prolonged the seance. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t suspect that the\r\ngirl was watching his face and listening for his words as one who\r\nwatches the judge\xe2\x80\x99s face and listens for the words which will restore\r\nhim to home and friends and freedom or shut him away from the sun and\r\nhuman companionship forever. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t suspect that his careless words\r\nwere being weighed, and so he often delivered sentence of death when it\r\nwould have been just as handy and all the same to him to pronounce\r\nacquittal. Daily he broke the girl\xe2\x80\x99s heart, nightly he sent her to the\r\nrack for sleep. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t understand it.\r\n\r\nSome people would have put this and that together and perceived that\r\nthe weather never changed until one particular subject was introduced,\r\nand that then it _always_ changed. And they would have looked further,\r\nand perceived that that subject was always introduced by the one party,\r\nnever the other. They would have argued, then, that this was done for a\r\npurpose. If they could not find out what that purpose was in any\r\nsimpler or easier way, they would _ask_.\r\n\r\nBut Tracy was not deep enough or suspicious enough to think of these\r\nthings. He noticed only one particular; that the weather was always\r\nsunny when a visit _began_. No matter how much it might cloud up later,\r\nit always began with a clear sky. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t explain this curious fact\r\nto himself, he merely knew it to be a fact. The truth of the matter\r\nwas, that by the time Tracy had been out of Sally\xe2\x80\x99s sight six hours she\r\nwas so famishing for a sight of him that her doubts and suspicions were\r\nall consumed away in the fire of that longing, and so always she came\r\ninto his presence as surprisingly radiant and joyous as she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t when\r\nshe went out of it.\r\n\r\nIn circumstances like these a growing portrait runs a good many risks.\r\nThe portrait of Sellers, by Tracy, was fighting along, day by day,\r\nthrough this mixed weather, and daily adding to itself ineradicable\r\nsigns of the checkered life it was leading. It was the happiest\r\nportrait, in spots, that was ever seen; but in other spots a damned\r\nsoul looked out from it; a soul that was suffering all the different\r\nkinds of distress there are, from stomach ache to rabies. But Sellers\r\nliked it. He said it was just himself all over\xe2\x80\x94a portrait that sweated\r\nmoods from every pore, and no two moods alike. He said he had as many\r\ndifferent kinds of emotions in him as a jug.\r\n\r\nIt was a kind of a deadly work of art, maybe, but it was a starchy\r\npicture for show; for it was life size, full length, and represented\r\nthe American earl in a peer\xe2\x80\x99s scarlet robe, with the three ermine bars\r\nindicative of an earl\xe2\x80\x99s rank, and on the gray head an earl\xe2\x80\x99s coronet,\r\ntilted just a wee bit to one side in a most gallus and winsome way.\r\nWhen Sally\xe2\x80\x99s weather was sunny the portrait made Tracy chuckle, but\r\nwhen her weather was overcast it disordered his mind and stopped the\r\ncirculation of his blood.\r\n\r\nLate one night when the sweethearts had been having a flawless visit\r\ntogether, Sally\xe2\x80\x99s interior devil began to work his specialty, and soon\r\nthe conversation was drifting toward the customary rock. Presently, in\r\nthe midst of Tracy\xe2\x80\x99s serene flow of talk, he felt a shudder which he\r\nknew was not his shudder, but exterior to his breast although\r\nimmediately against it. After the shudder came sobs; Sally was crying.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my darling, what have I done\xe2\x80\x94what have I said? It has happened\r\nagain! What _have_ I done to wound you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe disengaged herself from his arms and gave him a look of deep\r\nreproach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you done? I will tell you what you have done. You have\r\nunwittingly revealed\xe2\x80\x94oh, for the twentieth time, though I _could_ not\r\nbelieve it, _would_ not believe it!\xe2\x80\x94that it is not me you love, but\r\nthat foolish sham, my father\xe2\x80\x99s imitation earldom; and you have broken\r\nmy heart!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my child, what are you saying! I never dreamed of such a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Howard, Howard, the things you have uttered when you were\r\nforgetting to guard your tongue, have betrayed you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThings I have uttered when I was _forgetting_ to guard my tongue?\r\nThese are hard words. When have I _remembered_ to guard it? Never in\r\none instance. It has no office but to speak the truth. It needs no\r\nguarding for that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHoward, I have noted your words and weighed them, when you were not\r\nthinking of their significance\xe2\x80\x94and they have told me more than you\r\nmeant they should.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say you have answered the trust I had in you by using\r\nit as an ambuscade from which you could set snares for my unsuspecting\r\ntongue and be safe from detection while you did it? You have not done\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94surely you have not done this thing. Oh, one\xe2\x80\x99s enemy could not do\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was an aspect of the girl\xe2\x80\x99s conduct which she had not clearly\r\nperceived before. Was it treachery? Had she abused a trust? The thought\r\ncrimsoned her cheeks with shame and remorse.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, forgive me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI did not know what I was doing. I have\r\nbeen so tortured\xe2\x80\x94you _will_ forgive me, you _must;_ I have suffered so\r\nmuch, and I am so sorry and so humble; you _do_ forgive me, _don\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t turn away, don\xe2\x80\x99t refuse me; it is only my love that is at\r\nfault, and you _know_ I love you, love you with all my heart; I\r\ncouldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to\xe2\x80\x94oh, dear, dear, I am so miserable, and I _never_ meant\r\nany harm, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t see where this insanity was carrying me, and how\r\nit was wronging and abusing the dearest heart in all the world to\r\nme\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94oh, take me in your arms again, I have no other refuge, no\r\nother home and hope!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was reconciliation again\xe2\x80\x94immediate, perfect, all-embracing\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nwith it utter happiness. This would have been a good time to adjourn.\r\nBut no, now that the cloud-breeder was revealed at last; now that it\r\nwas manifest that all the sour weather had come from this girl\xe2\x80\x99s dread\r\nthat Tracy was lured by her rank and not herself, he resolved to lay\r\nthat ghost immediately and permanently by furnishing the best possible\r\nproof that he _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ have had back of him at any time the suspected\r\nmotive. So he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me whisper a little secret in your ear\xe2\x80\x94a secret which I have kept\r\nshut up in my breast all this time. Your rank _couldn\xe2\x80\x99t_ ever have been\r\nan enticement. I am son and heir to an English earl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe girl stared at him\xe2\x80\x94one, two, three moments, maybe a dozen\xe2\x80\x94then her\r\nlips parted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou?\xe2\x80\x9d she said, and moved away from him, still gazing at him in a kind\r\nof blank amazement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy\xe2\x80\x94why, certainly I am. Why do you act like this? What have I done\r\n_now?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you done? Then he said, in a discouraged way:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see any way out of it. It was a mistake. That is in truth all\r\nit was, just a mistake. No harm was meant, no harm in the world. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t see how it might some time look. It is my way. Show me the signs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\np243.jpg (29K)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank God I am not able\xe2\x80\x94if those are the signs. But yet I am an\r\nearl\xe2\x80\x99s son and heir. You\r\ndo not put your hand in your pocket _now_\xe2\x80\x94for you have nothing there.\r\nYou make a claim like this, and then venture to travel without\r\ncredentials. These are simply incredibilities. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see that,\r\nyourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe cast about in his mind for a defence of some kind or other\xe2\x80\x94hesitated\r\na little, and then said, with difficulty and diffidence:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you just the truth, foolish as it will seem to you\xe2\x80\x94to\r\nanybody, I suppose\xe2\x80\x94but it _is_ the truth. I had an ideal\xe2\x80\x94call it a\r\ndream, a folly, if you will\xe2\x80\x94but I wanted to renounce the privileges and\r\nunfair advantages enjoyed by the nobility and wrung from the nation by\r\nforce and fraud, and purge myself of my share of those crimes against\r\nright and reason, by thenceforth comrading with the poor and humble on\r\nequal terms, earning with my own hands the bread I ate, and rising by\r\nmy own merit if I rose at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl scanned his face narrowly while he spoke; and there was\r\nsomething about his simplicity of manner and statement which touched\r\nher \xe2\x80\x94touched her almost to the danger point; but she set her grip on\r\nthe yielding spirit and choked it to quiescence; it could not be wise\r\nto surrender to compassion or any kind of sentiment, yet; she must ask\r\none or two more questions. Tracy was reading her face; and what he read\r\nthere lifted his drooping hopes a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn earl\xe2\x80\x99s son to do that! Why, he were a man! A man to love!\xe2\x80\x94oh, more,\r\na man to worship!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he never lived! He is not born, he will not be born. A moment\xe2\x80\x94wait\xe2\x80\x94let me finish; I have one question more. Your\r\nfather is earl of what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRossmore\xe2\x80\x94and I am Viscount Berkeley!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe fat was in the fire again. I will bring you a message\xe2\x80\x94a cablegram\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo-morrow\xe2\x80\x94next day\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSigned \xe2\x80\x98Rossmore\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94signed Rossmore.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will that prove?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will it prove? Through her sobbings her words broke from time to\r\ntime.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, he is gone. I have lost him, I shall never see him any more.
